LONDON, Feb. 27.â The War Office has received the following dis
patch from Lord Roberts:
"PAARDEBERG, Feb. 27 (11 o'clock, Tuesday morning).â
From, information furnished to me daily by the intelligence department
it became apparent that General Cronje's force was becoming more de
pressed and that the discontent of the troops and the discord among the j
leaders vras rapidly increasing. This feeling was doubtless accentuated
by the disappointment caused when the Boer reinforcements which tried
to relieve General Crcnje were defeated by our troops on February 23.
I resolved, therefore, to bring pressure to bear upon the enemy. Each
night the trenches were pushed forward toward the enemy's. Jaager. so .aa^ _
to gracnallycontract his position and at the same time I bombarded.it
heavily with artillery, which was yesterday materially aided by the ar
rival of four six-inch howitzers which I ordered up from De Aar. In
isrrying out these measures a captive balloon gave great assistance by
seeping us informed of the disposition and movements of the enemy.
"At. 3 a. m. tc-day a most dashing advance was made by the Cana
dian regiment and some engineers, supported by the Gordon Highland
;rs and Second Shropshires, resulting in our gaining a point some 600
jrards rearer th; enemy and within about eighty yards of his trenches,
where our men entrenched themselves and maintained their positions
until morning, a gallant deed worthy of our colonial comrades, and which,
I am glad to say, was attended by comparatively slight loss.
"This apparently clinched matters, for at daylight to-day a letter
6ig-ned by Cronje, in which he stated that he surrendered uncondition
ally, was brought to our outposts under a flag of trues.
"In my reply I told General Cronje he must present himself at my
camp and that his forces must come out of "their laager after laying
down their arms. By 7 a. m. I received General Cronje and dispatched
a telegram to you announcing the fact. In the course of conversation he
asked for kind treatment at our hands, and also that his wife, grand
son, private secretary, adjutant and servants might accompany him
wherever he might be sent. I reassured him and told him that his request
would be complied with. I informed him that a r general officer would be
sent with him to Cape Town to insure his being treated with. proper re
spect en route. He will start this afternoon under charge of Major Gen
eral Pirttyman, who will hand him over 'to the general com man ding at
Cap: Town. Â¦Â¦;â¢Â¦# ""-. Â¦ - '
"The prisoners, who number about 3000, will be formed into
commandos under our own officers. They will also leave here to-day,
reaching the Modder River. to-morrow, when they will be railed to Cape
Town in detachments." . . . .
The above dispatch wns read in both the House of Lords and the
House of Commons to-day. The reference to the Canadians evoked im
mense and prolonged cheers.
A. J. Balfour, the Government leader in the House of Commons,
said he had no information relative to the Boer guns.

day morning by the Canadians to within eighty yards of the Boer trenches, and the enemy was
compelled to surrender.
All-important questions this morning are: ''What effect will the capitulation have upon the
Boers?" and "What will be Roberts' next move?" This British victory does not mean a collapse
on the part of the Boers. It means the freeing of the Free State forces by" the removal of ' one of
the most skillful commanders and the return to their farms of the weak-kneed, among the
Free Staters, but in the Free State one other seriesof actions at least will have to- be fought,
while the march into the Transvaal means very hard fighting and the loss of' many lives. That
the British Government appreciates this is shown by the announcement that the. Eighth Division
will be sent out so soon as transports are available. .
The consensus oi military opinion-here is that Roberts will; move on Bloemfontein
(French is reported to be already on the way), perhaps sending a strong mounted force to re
lieve Mafeking. It is not thought that- the Boers will make a' stand >t the Free State capital, as
it is not easily defended, but strong positions are to be found on the border of the; two republics.
Buller is finding Boer resistance to -his advance on Ladysmith ' increasing rather than
diminishing, and as yet White has not been able to assist in the movement. In fact "the Boers
are trying to. outflank the British, which attempt, if it succeeds, will" to a great extent justify the
result of the week's fighting. * : S'sf^

ENORMOUS DIFFICULTIES OF BULLER'S TASK.
LONDON, Feb. 28.â A dispatch to the Dally Mail from Pietermaritzberg. dated
Monday, describes at considerable lensth the "enormous difficulties of- General
Buller's task," and says: "The real advance began last Wednesday. The stu
pendous nature, of the task was only understood when, being on the spot, or.o
was - able to realize what it means to hurl Infantry at positions aligned in every
direction with carefully -prepared trenches and wi^h breastworks defended by
practically Invisible riflemen, armed' with "the most deadly rapid-firing weapons
and aided -by quick* firing guns which put all but our cannon of the lightest cal
ibre into the shade." ,;',,.>: ; ; " :
Commenting upon Friday's fighting, the 'Daily' Mall's correspondent says:
"As It would have been madness to seek an outlet from the amphitheater of hills
by way of Groblers Kloof, on the feft. a route had to be found on the right, and
at 2 p.m. on Friday,- amid the incessant crackling of rifle flre and the roar of
cannon', ; the "Irish, brigade was distributed along the river to take Railway Hill
on the other side 'of Pieters Station. This hill is commanding enough to ensure
our final advance, providing we could hold" it securely and could mount -cannon
on it. -From Onderbrook. Spruit railway bridge the track runs along the Tuge:a.
and it- was perilously open to the Eoer marksmen disposed on the kopjes back
from the river, and, after passing Pinters Station many men would be bound to
be hit. \ Beyond the station the. line passes .over another small bridge crossing a
deep donga., and' it was in the appro ich to this bridge and onward to the base
of Railway Hill that the greatest danger from enforced exposure obtained. It
was necessary to cross by the bridge at the mouth of the donga, which, besidfc*
being difficult of access, ran into the Tugela. The path between the. railway and
the river was almost always in fut! view of the enemy. Every man of the IrÂ»3h
brigade had, therefore, to run the ga*mtlet of Boer marksmen, and numbers
dropped on, the bridge where. the Boers' bullets, fell dangerously thick, until tha
bridge was sandbagged and only one man allowed on it at a time. Very soon fifty
men .were put out of action in the race from the bridge to the rendezvous. Bnl
several members â¢of the volunteer ambulance corps were wounded in following
the troops." ?vT *.-%<Â¦--â¢ :-':: -' : - â¢

DISASTROUS ATTEMPT TO CARRY RAILWAY HILL.
LONDON. Feb. 28.â The Dally Mall publishes this dispatch from Its special
correspondent. Richard. Harding Davis:
PIETERS, Feb. 24.â Last night the Innlskilllng Fusiliers, mixed with some
of the Dublin Fusiliers and Connaught Rangers, attempted to carry Railway
Hill from Pleters. They, were under a continuous .fire, through which they
passed up heavy, broken ground to the first Boer trench. Half way up the hill
the Boers . retreated to the crest and then came back on either flank of the Irish
troops, enfilading the captured , trench with a savage cross as well as a direct
fire. All night until 9 o'clock In the morning. the British held the position un
der a ceaseless' fire and at terrible cost. At roll call only five officers and one
hundred men answered to their names. Colonel Thackery and Major Sanders of
the Inniskillings. Colonel Sitwell of the Dublin Fusiliers, Captain Maitland of
the Second Gordons and Colonel Thorold of the -Welch Fusiliers were killed.
Two hundred and fifty-two of the rank and file were killed or wounded. General
Lyttelton's brigade relieved them at 10 o'clock this morning, when the Boer artil
lery, pounded the troops holding the center kopjes without pause, but with lit
tle, effect. Â¦:
CORRESPONDENT MURDERED BY AN OFFICER.
LONDON. Feb. 28.â Mr. -Parslow, the. correspondent of the Dally Chronicle at
Mafeklng," who 'had: previously been reported as accidentally shot and klllled on
November .2, : was. It appears from the diary of a Morning Post correspondent
and 'from, a letter received by the Daily Chronicle, murdered by Lieutenant
Murchison of the British artillery, reputed to be a man of fine professional abil
ities. It seems .t hat Mr., Parslow and Lieutenant Murchison had dined together
and that, when they were strolling across the square, Murchison was seen ta
draw a revolver. and to kill 'his companion. An inquiry was held and a finding of
willful murder was returned. Lieutenant Murchison was tried by court-martial.

LONDON, Feb. 28. â General .Cronje's unconditional surrender yesterday with 4000 men, in
cluding nearly fifty officers of important" rank and four big guns, is the triumphant, result of
Lord Roberts' strategy. It was a piece of brilliant soldiership,; strongly conceived and per
fectly executed. ' \\i?C-'
Quickly massing together two weeks ago a flying force of cavalry and mounted infantry
10,000 strong, with field guns, he brought up behind Moddcr River his infantry division. Then
he sent French with clouds of cavalry under ordersjto. sweep over, the drifts to Kiniberley, reliev
ing that place.
Cronje found himself outflanked. If he retreated north Methuen and French would have
enveloped him. He was obliged to. give up Kiniberley and ilee to the eastward. On.the night of
the 15th he started on a forced march toward Paardeberg. Accomplishing thirty-tHree miles; ; : in|
one desperate march, he was discerned on the morning of the 1 6th and followed up sharply by the
cavalry. Lord Kitchener and General Kelly-Kenny' caught up with his rear guard, driving it. in
upon the main body, which was outspanned with cattle, weary. and tired men in the bed of the river.
There he was penned in on the 18th. Roberts, at the head of the infantry division, sealed him
up between Koodoosberg and Paardeberg in a trap. -For a whole week the Boer commandos
were shut up between the river banks and stormed at day and; night with . artillery and rifle fire.
Roberts held off the scattered reinforcements and maintained the close, unsparing fire,
which rendered Cronje's position untenable. Finally a dashing advance was made early yester-

Situation at Hilo.
"\\ nether there is plague at HIIo or not;
whether or not there ever may be plague
there, need not affect cne particle of the
traffic of the Hawaiian capital." said Con
eul Haywood. "I was discouraged when I
left here for Hilo, but I return very much

ransre and carry out any scheme made
necessary by events by correspondence,
"I found the people there perfectly calm
and working together to the best possible
end. There was no shotgun business
there, and I do not believe there ever has
been. We were met at the wharf by Mr.
Lyman. There was no appearance af ex
citement of any kind and certainly no in
dication of trouble, past or present.
"I do hope there will not be an epidemic
of plague. at liilo. At the same time, the
scare has done lots of good. In that the
town has been cleaned as it had never
been before. People have worked together
and untiringly to this end. and, though
I had not been there in two years I
could see the result instantly. The situa
tion then* Is all right, and I feel good."

"BRAVO, BOBS,"
AND "MAJUBA"
ARE THE CRIES
LONDON, Feb. 27.â "Majuba Avenged,"
"Cronje Surrenders," "Great British Vic
tory-"" These are the expressions shout
ed to-day. The capitulation -of General
Cronje had been looked upon as almost a
certainty for a week past and now that It
has come enthusiasm finds itself discount
ed by the anticipation.
The magnitude of the success of "Lit
tle Bobs" is almost overlooked in the fact
that it synchronized with the anniversary
of Majuba Hill and wipedout a dishonor
of nineteen years' standing. While' the
afternoon papers all comment upon this
happy coincidence, they do not forget the
splendid bravery of the^enemy. -"'.-..
The St. Janies Gazette .says: *
"The splendid courage of the Boers has
not been able to withstand any longer the
bombardment which few modern soldiers
have supported for as many, hours as
they -have days, 'The Â¦influence of the
surrender, not only upon. the situation. at
Ladysmlth, but on the' whole conduct/of
the war, will be immense. 7 If the news, of
the relief -of .Ladysmlth jj only breaches
England, and It is quite/po'sstblcfhis may
be the case,. the cup. pt national' happiness
would ; be .full," : ?/:â¢ ;?" '!*""' r - " Â¦" V ; ,
"'The' Globe, under; the heading of "Ma-,
juba Avenged," says:' r .. "â¢ ;, ;,'; ."..
. "We shall not jump at the conclusion
that the war is over, but that a victory
has been< gained ; which brings tbeiend
well in 'sight. General Cronje . no doubt
succeeded In delaying Lord Roberts'. Â¦ eri^
trance Into^ Bloemfontein, -but the delay.
Is all' he has been able to accomplish." Â¦
The Westminster Gazette, whlch.is In
clined to; be pro-Boer, 1 says: , - 'Â¦Â¦','.' ,
"Admiration for the desperate valor of
General Cronje is mingled with anger at
thenepeasity Imposed upon us of purchas
ing victory at this cost. Let us remember
Paardeberg if we like; but forget Majuba
and have ; done with the whole j train jof
misleading associations - and "mischievous
ideas which gathered around that un
happy memory."'.
No one who did -not know of General
Cronje's surrender would suspect from
the appearance of the town that
London was to-day, celebrating the chief
victory of the war. Not a flag is flying
and in front of the places where bulletins
are displayed to the public scarcely more
than a dozen people stop at a time to read
the news. Outside of the War Office there
is the same absence of enthusiasm, which
is due doubtless to the drizzling rain.
In the War Office lobbies, when a* clerk
posted the news of the' surrender. quite a
crowd was waiting, and here the tidings
were hailed with considerable enthusiasm,
and in an incredibly short space of time
the news had spread to all parts of Lon
don. At the Mansion House when the
news was posted It was received- with
great excitement by the crowds of busi
ness men hurrying to their offices, and
cries of "Bravo, Roberts!" and "Majuba!"
were heard.
The Lord Mayor of London said to the
correspondent of the ." Associated Press
that while the surrender, of ; General
Cronje was a foregone conclusion, the
news was received with a great sense of
relief, and the result of the capitulation
he thought would be the rapid conclusion
of hostilities.
At the Stock Exchange there was much
excitement and cheering, but this quickly
subsided when the house opened. Stocks
opened with an upward tendency, but no
material Improvement In prices and con
sols strangely were slightly weaker.
The news of General Cronje's surrender
was received with unbounded satisfaction
at Windsor. The. Queen immediately tel
egraphed her congratulations to Lord
Roberts and the troops.
There was a much 'more outward ' dlp
play of rejoicing in the provinces than in
London. The Glasgow and' Liverpool
Stock Exchanges were greatly, excited.
The members sang "God Save the QueÂ«u"
repeatedly, and cheered for ."Little. Bobs."
Flags were liberally displayed on the
municipal and house buildings in all the
large towns.
. Windsor was very : jubilant at the news,
which the Queen disseminated immediate
ly after it reached her, * and " the i inhabi-
tants seized the occasion of Her. Majesty
driving through; the town on* her way to
take a train for a visit to' the wounded
South African soldiers at the Netley Hos
pital to give vent to their delight.
The Queen, who' was looking.remarka
bly.-; well, showed evident signs of deep
satisfaction, smiling and continually bow
ing in response to the congratulatury
cheers greeting her on all sides. Her
Majesty took quantities of flowers to dis
tribute to the wounded. '
All the garrison towns and naval norts
received the news with the liveliest satis
faction, soldiers and sailors-) vying with
each other in displays of delight. ,
. Immense crowds awaited the \ Queen at
Netley and. cheered themselves hoarpe.

CLOSING HOURS
OF PORTO RICAN
TARIFF DEBATE
Friends and Foes of the Bill
Match Oratory in the
House.
Argument Embraces All Phases of
Expansion, Democrats Protest
ing Against the Holding of
the Philippines.
WASHINGTON. Feb. 27.â The general
debate' on the Porto Rico tariff bill closed
to-day in a blaze of glory. The galleries
were crowded to the doors and every seat
on the floor was occupied when the rival
champions of the' respective sides, Dolli
ver of lowa and Bailey of Texas, made the
closing arguments. Each spoke an hour
and a half. The speech of Bailey, devot
ed as it was almost exclusively to the
legal phases of. the. controversy,, while. It
was profound and impressive, did not
arouse the unbounded enthusiasm which
swept the galleries and floors while Dolli
ver was speaking. The lowan was at his
best and his wit, eloquence and sarcasm
in turn drew salvos of applause from his
Republican associates. Before the closing
speeches were made Cannon of Illinois,
chairman -of â¢ the Appropriations -Commit
tee and "one of the veterans "on the Repub
lican side, made an exceptionally effective
speech in support of the. bill. Carmarck
(D.)- of : Tennessee, Clayton (D.) of Ala
bama, Kloberg (D.) of Texas, Pearce and
De Armond (D.) of Missouri made
speeches in opposition to the bill. The
Republican managers are now : confident
that with the modifications agreed upon
at the conference la9t night the bill will
command a narrow majority In the final
vote to-morrow. Â¦ Â¦ '
Clayton of Alabama created much
amusement and enthusiasm by reading a
letter written by Chairman Payne to W.
H. Curtis of Palmyra. N, V., on January
24, 1900, in which he advocated free trade
with Porto Rico on . the ground that it
would give relief to "the stricken island.
The latter contained the following refer
ence to the Philippines:
Under the treaty the Philippine Islands are
to have free trade with Spain for ten years.
We couM not extend these, tariff laws to the
Philippine Islands, even if we would.
Clayton asked that this letter and an
extract from Mr. Payne's speech in sup
port of the bill.be printed in parallel col
umns in the record. Payne smilinely as
sented to the request, and expressed the
hop* that there would be no objection.
Peary of Tennessee declared that the
brood of trusts had flocked to Washing
ton and had compelled the leaders of the
Republican party to turn their backs
upon Porto Rico. He asked if the people
of that island were not entitled to as
much consideration as the Sultan of Sulu.
who was granted free trade with all the
Philippine Islands.
Philippine Policy Denounced.
De Armond of Missouri said this bill pro
posed to set aside the constitution not for
the purpose of doing something philan
thropic, but in the interest of the partial
or complete enslavement of a helpless
people.
"This change of front," he declared,
"has been brought about by the influ
ence of the agents of mighty corpora-
He said the administration had deter
mined blindly and greedily to hold the
Philippines for what could be made out
of them. It mattered little about Porto
Rico. The Porto Ricans were to be cruci
fied in order that the cross mieht tee
raised in the Philippines. -We could with-,
draw from the archipelago and leave
10.000,000 tropical people to work out their
own salvation. . !
"In my opinion," said he, "that is what
we ought to do." . (Democratic applause.)
He thought the time was coming when
there would be a revulsion in the Repub
lican party, when the" Republicans who
now had the backbone to oppose the pres
ent programme would be hailed as the ;
saviors of their party.
Cannon of Illinois followed De Armond. :
He scouted the cry that had been raised :
that the trusts had dictated the bill. He [
charged that both the tobacco and sugar
stored in Porto Rico was already owned .
by the - sugar and tobacco trusts. They
had. put forward some natives as stool
pigeons to beg for free trade, so that
I while the Democrats cried out against the
trusts they would be voting for them.
"Their voice would be the voice of Jacob,
but their hand would be the hand of
Esau."
Bailey- of Texas made an exhaustive
argument against the constitutionality of
the bill,* arousing the lawyers on the
other side so that* fora time he was al
most .continuously engaged in crossing
swords; with one or the other of his ad
versaries. . . ' .... \ .
.'. Dolliver Closes the Debate.
D.olliver :ot'lowa: ot'Iowa closed the debate for
the. majority. â¢:, Said he :
;ThJs "bill' la'Voerely a' temporary â¢ measure to
provide' revÂ«tue' Tor . Porto.- Rico. .Yet .it hae:

It was declared that this bill was in
behalf -of- -the sugar trust, when the fact
.was, declared - Dolliver. that -the sugar
trust, wanted free, trade. The-same was
true of the tobacco trust. He continued?
â¢ If the constitution of the United States es
tablishes free Â¦ trade between Manila and Saa
Francisco, .;**Â¦. it undoubtedly does between
Maryland and Virginia, then the fact that 'we,
have got Into a. new competition is only the.
leant trouble that has been before us. We are
in that case disarmed at once in the momentous;
demand which this nation has .made for equal;
rights Jn the â¢ commerce of Asia.' I. violate, no
secret r of . this Government when I say that to
day there Is pending in the State -Department
the most- elaborate commercial negotiation ever
attempted in the history of our diplomacy,
based on a, pledge made by our commissioners
at- Paris that whatever we did in the Pacific
Ocean we wculd maintain an open door for the

great cotton belt of the South who are looking
forward to the opportunities that are wrapped
up In our commerce in the Pacific Ocean in

der being carried away by our own constitu

community of American business, North and
South, looks forward to opportunities not even

broaden with the centuries, that is the real
question that is on trial here.
Dolliver then defended .the President's
course in the Philippines, read General
Lawton's letter charging that the Insur
rection in the Philippines was kept alive
by the agitation in this country and urged
his countrymen, North and South, with
out regard to their political party, "to put
a stop to the unscrupulous agitation that
for nearly two years has filled the hearts
of our own boys with despair and the
hearts of their enemies with confidence
and good cheer." He concluded:
O my countrymen, we are In the midst of
euch difficult responsibilities that I sometimes
feel that our resolution and our purpose are
almost overwhelmed. â¢ â¢ â¢ There are two
ideas of the national duty In this emergency.
One is that the nation should take care of It
self, and let the world alone. That is a nar
row view of our national duty. -The other ideal
of national life, an Ideal born In the heart of
the workman of Nazareth, is that no nation
liveth to Itself alone, but that every real na
tional life pours Itself out In the service of
mankind. I don't believe that the "American
republic, wlih a motive like that and a pur
pose like that, will be allowed by Providence
to fall amid the difficulties of this hour.
You have read the mastemlece â¢ of fiction
where a hand from the sky touches the shoulder
of Napoleon the Great, puts an end to his
career, and nays to him: "Tour business is
over, your time is up; your affairs are at an
end." That was the gleam of a superb Imagi
nation. There is In this world the steady light
of a faith more superb even than that, a faith
in the future of humanity, a faith in the great
ness of the republic, a faith in Providence, who
has guided every footstep of our national Iff e
from Infancy until this hour.
Let us have faith in God and faith In ' the
American republic and In our future mission.
I believe In the United States' of America. I
Hack the old republic of 'our â¢ fathers against
the world. Nor. do I think that since the good
days of Abraham Lincoln 1 there has been upon
the helm of our affairs a steadier,' wiser, kinder,
braver hand than the- hand of William Me-
Xl nicy. President of the United States. '

The, government which was organized for the

It has been repeatedly said here' that govern
ment by the United States in these Islands will
be a despotism. Such a belief shows a singular
ignorance. or misapprehension of the constitu
tional limitations upon our powers and the
spirit of our Institutions. While .the consti
tution does not extend over the territories of
Its own force and without legislation. Its pro
hibitions are binding on Congress. In- those
prohibitions, which are also privileges enjoyed
by the people- wherever our. Jurisdiction 1 ex
tends, is a complete charter 'of rights, which
Congress. can neither limit nor impair. All per
sonal privileges, and immunities, such as reli
gious freedom,', property rights, freedom of
speech and freedom of the preus and equality
before the law,; must* prevail wherever our flag
floats. .But outside of the constitution and laws
is an unwritten law,- created by the genius of
the .institutions of the paramount power, and
controlling its acts and officials in all colonial
governments. -Â¦-Â¦-.â¢ .

Hawaii's' Contract Labor.
Consideration of the Hawaii govern
ment bill was then resumed. The contract
labor question was precipitated again by

been imported. to Hawaii; their contracts
extending' over -periods of .from three to
ten years.' , ' "
In . response . to questions as to whether
Asiat'lv contract laborers displaced local
laborers, Pettigrew replied that to - some
extent they did. .He went on to say that
the coffee lands, of which we have heard
so much, practically, had been changed
Into sugar plantations, the lands as such
being Immensely productive. These sugar
plantations were controlled by vast sugar
interests, which during the past year had
been paid by this Government in remitted
duties - $12,000,000. These great interests
would control the. Legislature of Hawaii

The costumes and dresses at the Mardi
Gras ball, were the finest ever seen at any
event of the kind here.
Mrs. Mitchell Phillips and Mrs. E. O.

mittee in charge, cleared over $1200 . by
the ball. The proceeds of the ball will
go to erect a building to house the magni
ficent art collection given to the people by-
Mrs: M. P. O'Connor. The returns of to-
night will give the committee of ladies
who have the matter entirely in their
hands a total of about $10,000. It is pro
posed, to raise about the same amount in
addition and erect- a suitable building In
Normal Square.
STORM OF CONFETTI
ON PARIS STREETS
PARIS, Feb.. 27.â A drenching rain and
a leaden sky this afternoon threatened to
make the fate of Mardl Gras a complete
failure, : so' far as Paris Is concerned.
Nevertheless a pood number of people
gathered along the boulevards, where con
fetti tables were allowed, and, splashing
through mud and rain, exchanged volleys
of these fatal missiles. As evening ap
proached, â¢ however, the rain ceased.
Crowds flocked to the Avenue dcs Champs
Elysees and the boulevards, where all the
stores, were closed, and soon the tradi
tional gayety of the Parisian asserted it
self and King Carnival reigned supreme.
From one end of Paris to the other the air
dally in front of the cafes, from which, col
was filled .with clouds of confetti, espe
ored serpentines were thrown in return.
A living sea of merrymakers'thronged the
principal thoroughfares, and the sido
walks and , roadways were 'Inches deep
with confetti, as though Paris had passed
through a variegated snowstorm. â :Â¦

The bal masquÂ« of the Art Associa

the annual society/event, although fate
and the board of directors of the asso

eth human understanding. In great
families renowned for intellectual pres
tige genius frequently skips a gener
ation, hence the Art Association may
skip a ball in purely natural sequence.
The ball, therefore, annual, biennial
or triennial, always engages the atten
tion of society. Dowagers, maidens
and those of the fair sex in intermediate
grades of loveliness and beauty, are
fond of dismissing the prospective
charms and pleasure of the event. The
success of the function is not always as
sured by the simple announcement that
the Art Association has resolved to lend
its fame and protection to the ball. The
inflexible dowager, rejoicing in the title
of art patroness, often has a mind of her
sweet and inspiring music, the . pleas
ures of imagination, and they -almost
participated in a riot to get supper. But
all the time the Queen of Propriety,
jeweled and grand, in her. private box,

XIV. Books which society maidens of
high degree are not permitted to
peruse ; leave it an open question
whether this particular court which
;Peck .-. and Fletcher and Greenway
elected to reproduce was all that a
highly respectable and prudent court
should be.
The court knew itself, last night and
behaved . itself and was happy in the
favor of society.

Deeley's Victims Discharged.
C. A. Ensign. William F. Smith and C.
Benson, who were arrested on Monday
morning at Third and Stevenson streets
by Policeman L. J. Deeley on the chargÂ«
of Interfering with an officer, appeared
before Judge Cabanlss yesterday. They
were represented by Colonet E. F. Pres
ton and Fisher Ames.
Deeley testified that the- reason he mada
the arrests was because the defendant*
interfered with him In the performance
of his duty.
Besides the defendants' testimony, John-
M. Furlon*. Harry Clark. J. Casey and
Frank E. Kane all testified that the ar
rests were unwarranted and that Deeley
used vulgar language and conducted him
self like a man under the influence of
liquor. The Judge promptly dismissed
the cases. â¢
The attorneys for the defendants an
nounced that formal charges would be
preferred against Deeley before the Police
Commissioners. r .â¢ . Â¦ â¢

HEALTH BOARD DELAYS
SWINGING OF THE AX
Will Meet Mayor and Supervisors'
Finance Committee To-Day â Ap
propriation May Be Raised.
The Board of Health met last night with
the Intention of abolishing all Its depart
ments except the Harbor and Emergency
hospitals and dismissing all Its employes
except about s^ven, as already enumer
ated: but action was postponed until the
meeting to be held to-night. Dr. William
son announced that the Mayor and the
Finance Committee would meet the board
some time to-day for the purpose of
straightening out. if possible, the diffi
culties that now confront the board.
Dr. Williamson stated that he did not
care to prognosticate what the action of
the committee would be, but lt Is believed
that the members of the Board of Super
visors, with the possible exception of
Reed, have begun to realize the danger to
the health Interests of the city by a prac
tical abolition of the department and that
the Finance Committee Is prepared to rec
ommend an addition to the J25.000 already
appropriated. Whether that sum will be
s-foOO In order to place the appropriation
at the old fissure of $35,000, has not yet
been determined, but lt Is quite certain
that an Increase will be recommended.
The following committees were appoint
ed to carry out the provisions of Â¦ the
charter regarding the administration of
the Health Department:

SOCIETY RESENTS
GROSS INSULT TO
ITS FESTIVAL
AN afternoon newspaper Impressed
the disagreeable fact of Its existence
upon the public yesterday by offerlnij
a gratuitous and particularly offensive In
sult to everjMnan and woman who at
tended the ball. ~ln announcing the great
pleasure gathering of the Art Association
this sheet published an Indecent picture
of a woman, garbed in tights and an ex
cuso for draperies, and made the brutal
R!OT OP BEAUTY TN THE SUOW OF A THOUSAND LAMPS
TJÂ±"iS SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, FEBRUARY 28, 1900.
and utterly unjustified announcement that
many women would be similarly un
clothed at the festivities of the evening.
The offense Is immeasurably aggravated
by the fact that the annual masquerade
of the association is and has been abso
lutely above reproach. Its success has en
listed the energies of the best men and
women of San Francisco. Its festivities
have been marked by scenes that could
give no suggestion of impropriety to the
most evil minded. Its revelers and merry
makers have been men who value tneir
names and cherish the honor of their
women. Its supporters and well-wishers
have been those who have by right of
conduct' and honest lives a claim to re
spect and decent consideration. It is re
grettable to the last degree, therefore, that
the pruriency of an irresponsible sheet
should have the license to spread its in
decency before the public.

The army and navy officers Joined me
civil throng last night In paying tribute to
the genius of art. The glitter of golden
braid and the luster of brass buttons
added to the grandeur of the scene.
When it came to dancing and Jesting with
the fair sex the army was not shy and
neither was the navy timid.
MIDNIGHT SAW
THE SCENE AND
SOFTLY SMILED
AT MIDNIGHT all the old-time ball
goers, as well as the youthful pa
trons (jf the dance, testified that the
seventh, Mardi Gras -was the success of
the season. As early as U 'o'clock. . the
managers began to persuade, the. bankers,
merchants and professional men of middle
and advanced age that It was time they
were going to supper. The plain Inference
was that youth was eager for larger danc
ing space in the Jester's room and art gal
lery.
When the masks were thrown aside at
midnight there were the usual surprises
of the bal masque.
Caterer Wheeler managed his depart
ment of the functions with. ability and
served the array of Prince Carnival in
good style.
Fortune seemed to smile on the carni
val from the time of the grand march on
to the "sma' hours." - The" Â¦ thousand
lights, under the careful supervision of
electricians, did not fail. The number of
tickets sold, from which the association
receives $5 for each "one, was 760. This
showing gratified the president and the
directors.
As the night wore on the music became
more animated. The supper, too, had an
enlivening effect. There was a promise of
fun, something touching the innocent riot
of the carnival season.

CHANGES OF
BENEFIT TO
THE ARMY
Secretary Root's Appeal on
Behalf of the Military
Bill.
TO IMPROVE THE SERVICE
Lessons Taught by the War With
Spain Used to Good Advantage
in the Framing of the
Measure.
WASHINGTON. Feb. Z7. â Secretary
Root to-day appeared berore the House
Committee on Military Affairs and made
a strong appeal to the committee to re
port favorably the bill drafted by him. to
Increase the efficiency oÂ£ the army. The
Secretary said In part:
The bill is limited to three subjects. (1) Pro
motion in the line: <Z) tenure of etaS appoint
ments and the relations between the staff and
the line; (3) reorganization of the artillery.
It Is carefully framed to avoid raising the
QUeeticn ef the sixe cf tbe army, which, it is
understood. Congress Is not now ready to dis
cuss, and its provisions are made applicable
to any army of whatever slie Congress may
determine upon.
Tbe provision as to line promotion is that
every third promotion to the office of major,
lieutenant colonel and oolcnel shall be by se
lection, leaving the other two-thirds to be as
at present, by seniority. This Is designed to
afford some alight optxjrtunity for recognition
of exceptional ability and gallantry. Under
the present law, which permits promotion
only by seniority, the President is prohibited
from reaognlring meritorious or gallant con
duct: tbe most effective and brilliant service
must go unrewarded, the thoughtful, devoted
and ambitious young man Is precluded from
advancing himself by his own exertion a hair's
breadth beyond the indolent, the dull and the
Inefficient man who iÂ« Just able to perform
duty to keep from being turned out of the
service.
Guarding Against Favoritism.
The only objections to the proposed measur.
arÂ» that there may be favoritism in the selec
tions. The impartiality of the selection Is safe
guarded by the provision of the bill that the
promotion shall be made from a list of three
officers selected from the grade from which
the promotion is to be made by a board of
officers of superior rank convened for that
purpose and sworn to make the selection ac
cording to the merits and fitness of the com
mandant a* ehown by hie service records.
From these three the President is to make
the nomination, which has again to pat's the
ordeal of confirmation by the Senate. The of
ficers of the army have its efficiency and honor
and Justice to Their fallows more deeply at
heart than any one else in the world, and this
bill puts Into their hands tbe power and im
poses upon them the solemn duty of confining
these promotions strictly within the lines of
Justice.
The sections relating to staff organizations
provide for the abolition of the present sys
tem of permanent staff appointments and for
the substitution of details from the line or the
staff for periods of four years, unless sooner
terminated by the President, with the require
ment that upon the expiration of a detail the
officer shall serve at least one year in the line
before he Is eligible for another detail. The
advantages of these provisions. I believe, will
be manifold. I think the change will break up
and prevent the tendency to bureaucratic gov
ernment, of which there Is always danger, and
will increase the efficiency of both the line and
the staff, by breaking down the division be
tween them, by giving to each a more intimate
and personal knowledge and appreciation of
the difficulties and the needs of the other, and
by creating a larger body of all-around men

trained In every branch 1 of the service and

Efficiency of Bureau Chiefs.
The provision which permits thÂ« President to
terminate a detail to the staff is of tbe high
est importance. Nothing could be more un
practical than the present permanent tenure
cf the chief of a bureau. The President, as
Commander-in-chief, ia responsible for the ef
ficiency of the army. In time of war he 1Â«
expected by the country to produce results by
Its use. He can determine who shall command
the armies and can change generals in com
mand as often as he chooses, but he is abso
lutely powerless as to the heads of these great
departments which organize and assemble and
feed and clothe and transport and arm and
equip and care for the army. Victory, or, de
feat is organized in the staff departments be
fore a shot Is fired. Inefficiency in them means
necessary disaster at the beginning and the
expenditure of countless treasure and the sac
rifice of countless lives. Yet no matter how
plainly inadequate to the task the head of one
of these departments may be. the President it
powerless to make a change unless the officer
commits itome distinct and heinous offense
upon which he can be court-martialed and dis
missed from the service. |
The Secretary said care had been taken
to protect the rights of incumbents of
staff positions, and he understood the
great majority of them were' satisfied to
accept the change cheerfully. Their op
position, he said, was purely personal and
of the same kind which for years had un
dermined every attempt at improving
army organizations. Such considerations
should not prevail.
He then explained the provisions of the
bill in detail, dwelling especially on those
relating to the artillery. An increase of
6500 men. almost all In the coast artillery,
had been provided for, but the increase
would be spread over five years, and in
order to avoid raising any question as to
the size of our army at this time, the
1300 increase each year would be made up
by recruiting that many fewer in other
arms of the service. The regimental or
ganization Â¦ was abandoned as to the ar
tillery, and the battery' or company
adopted as the unit of organization, sub
stantially all military authorities being
agreed that there was only a multiplica
tion # of red tape, and no sense, in trying
to administer the artillery arm on a regi
mental basis when only one battery as a
rule was In one locality. A chief of ar
tillery was provided in order that this,
like other important branches, should
have a responsible head.
Some Opposition Develops.
Members of the committee asked many
questions and showed, great Interest in
the Secretary's statement. The provision
giving the adjutant general of the army
the rank of major general developed some
opposition, and Marsh of Illinois moved
to strike it out, but the motion did not
prevail. Section 2, which is Intended to
appply to General Shafter. again brought
out a motion by Jett of Illinois to strike
out, which also failed. The bill as. a
whole was then agreed to by a divided
vote. The first section abolishes post
chaplains, and authorizes the President to
appoint a chaplain for each regiment, reg
ular and volunteers. The other section is
as follows:
That the President Is hereby authorized to
eelet-t from the retired list of the army an of
ncer not above the rank of brigadier general,
who m&v have distinguished himself during
the war with Spain, in command of a separate
army, and to appoint, by and with the advice
and consent of the Senate, the officer co se
lected to be major general United States army,
Â¦with the pay and allowance established by law
for officers of that grade on the retired list.
That en and after the pat-pap? of this act
the adjutant ceneral of the United Stales army
fhall have the rank, pay and allowanced of a
major general.

all other regiments who were in service

HORRIFYING
SCENES AT A
TRAIN WRECK
Women Burned to Death
in Full View of '
Special Dispatch to The Call.
KANSAS CITY. Mo.. Feb. 27.â The bliz
zard prevailing throughout Western Mis
souri was the cirect cause of a train
wreck to-night on the main line of the
Missouri Pacific Railroad, In which at
least two persons and possibly more were
killed and several others were badly hurt.
The fast St. Louis day express, due to
arrive in Kansas City at 5:45 this evening,
was delayed by a freight train which
stuck in a snow drift two miles south of
Independence, Mo., about twelve' miles out
of Kansas City. The St. Louis local pas
senger train, running forty minutes beiiind
the fast express, came on through the
blinding snowstorm and crashed into the
express train ahead, the engineer having
failed in the driving snow to see the dan
ger signal which the first train had sent
back.
Such was the force of the collision that
the parlor car, which was In the rear of
the first train, was literally cut In two.
When Engineer" Frank Raymond and his
fireman escaped from the wreck they
crawled out through the windows of the
parlor car. Fire added to the horrors of
the wreck, coals from the furnace of the
shattered engine having fallen among the
debris of the splintered coach, and soon
the whole wreck was ablaze. Two or more
persons, it 1b believed, were burned. A ;let
of the dead and seriously injured, so far
as known, ie as follows:
Dead:
MRS. J. G. SCHMIDLAPP, Cincinnati;
instantly killed; body recovered.
UNKNOWN WOMAN; body consumed
in wreck.
Injured: J. G. Schmidlapp, Cincinnati,
will recover. Miss Schmidlapp, Cincinnati,
scalded; will lose sight of both eyes.
Mrs. J. Balke, Cincinnati; mother of
Mrs. Schmidlapp, badly scalded, eyesight
lost, but may recover. W. R. Vaughn,
Cincinnati; newspaper reporter; scalded
and right arm crushed; amputation neces
sary. L*. F. Sheldon, Sedalia; assistant
superintendent of Missouri Pacific, pain
fully scalded. Braketnan Frank McAfee,
St. Louis; badly bruised. Mrs. Elizabeth
Peters, Kansas City; scalded. Mrs. Eliza
beth Lee, Cincinnati; scalded.
All of the injured have been brought to
the University Hospital in Kansas City.
William Rost. a farmer, near whose
place thÂ« wreck occurred, and who was
one of the first to render any assistance
to the Imperiled passengers, is quite- sure
that at least three women were burned in
the wreck. When he reached the car
flames were crackling through the splint
ered woodwork of the car at one end,
while at the other a cloud of blistering
steam was belching from the locomo
tive, which had ripped the coach open
from end to end. On every side were
men and women crying for assistance.
Mr. Rost's first act was to pull from the
wreck a woman whose legs were sticking
out through a broken window. She was
not badly hurt. By the time this had
been accomplished other passengers from
the forward coaches had come back and
helped out all of those in the burning car
who could be reached.
Mr. Rost states that he saw the body
of one woman jammed in the roof of the
burning coach, and that it was not
reached by the rescuers. The body of an
other woman was consumed in full view
of the passenger* who gathered about the
wreck.
Mr. Rost and others tried to drag her
out, but she was pinioned under heavy
wreckage. Mr. Rost says the young wo
man was apparently dead, as he reached
her hand and there was no response to
his efforts at rescue. He describes the
woman as about 25 years of age. She
wore a dark dress and a leather chate
laine hung from her waist.
Mr. Rost believes that he saw the body
of still a third woman wedged between
timbers of theÂ» burning coach, but before
he could get closer the fire and smoke and
steam obscured his view.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, FEBRUARY 28, 1900.
GIVES LIFE
AS THE WAGE
OF HER SIN
Love Tragedy Back of the
Suicide of Bessie Evans Â¦
at Madera.
BUSINESS MAN DENOUNCED
Citizens in Mass-Meeting Adopt Res
olutions Branding Louis Kutner
as a Moral Coward and So
cial Outcast.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MADERA, Feb. 27.â Bessie* Evans, a
popular young waitress employed. at the
Yosemite Hotel, swallowed an ounce of
carbolic acid last Tuesday morning and
ran Into the room of Louis Kutner, a
prominent business man, where she died
a few minutes later. At first the fact of
her having died in the young man's room
was regarded as a mere accident, but
upon closer investigation evidence was
discovered which appeared to argue more
than that.
Two notes were found, both of which
had been written by the girl and ad
dressed to Kutner., In the first she said
that she loved him and that if it were not
for him she would not kill herself. The
second note was written on the back of a
postal card and was a trite quotation to
the effect that "chastity is the most
priceless g^ft that God has given to wo
man, and it should be returned to Him
unsullied." This note was signed with
the girl's initials, "B. E."
The Coroner's jury investigated the
case and examined several witnesses. It
read the notes written by the girl and ad
dressed to Kutner. The inquest resulted
in a verdict of suicide, the jury failing to
fix any responsibility.
When the notes were read at the in
quest and other evidence brought out. the
facts created a sensation. A number of
the society women of the town, headed by
Mrs. Dr. Butin, held a meeting at which
it was decided to issue a call for a mass
meeting of citizens to take place at the
Presbyterian Church last night. Fully
200 of Madera's citizens were in attend
ance and the discussion was of the most
animated description. The facts in the
case were reviewed and young Kutner
was denounced. A set of resolutions was
finally adopted without dissent, in which
the young business man was declared to
be morally responsible for the death of
the girl. Some of the most radical por
tions are as follows:
Kutner Denounced.
"Whereas. An innocent youne girl of excellent
reputation (by name Bessie Evans) having been
betrayed by flattering promises and the hope
of a future happy home into an unholy alliance
with a moral coward, not to say libertine <by
name Louis Kutner), who by his relationship
with her and the conditions Into which he
wantonly hurled her has by these misdeeds
caused her to seek and find a suicide's jrrave
unavenged. Therefore be It
Resolved. That we, the citizens of Madera, in
mass meeting assembled, in the name of moral
purity, home and Justice, do hereby call upon
all to â¢whom these presents may come to use
every moral influence to erase from our midst
the name of Louis Kutner, whem we brand as
a social outcast.
ReÂ«olved. That we view with consternation
the attitude of the Madera Times In this case.
in which It makes an effort to gloss over this
crime and by innuendo to blacken the reputa
tion of the dead girl. We believe that where
a newsDaoer tries to condone such a crtme as
this it Is not worthy of the, respect or support
of the public.
The next resolution asks the Board of
Supervisors , of. -Madera- County to with
draw all the county patronage from the
Times. The paper Is a -weekly and is
edited by J. E. Nugrent. It suppressed the
sensational features of the suicide in its
account of the affair, and defended Kut
ner. Ine Mercury, another weekly, takes
the side of the people as represented in
the mass meeting.
The suicide and its consequences have
worked up the people of Madera to a
state of high excitement. Kutner has
left town and it Is said that he is in San
Francisco.

HER MAJESTY'S FLAG.
CECIL RHODES, saved from the Boers by the
relief of Kimberley at. the expense of great
treasure and the life of many, a British -soldier,
has taken occasion, at a meeting of the De Beers
Company to speak out of the fullness of his heart and
exult in the victory. Coming from >such a, man , at
such a time,- the speech, was certain to" be, widely;re
ported, and Rhodes must" have known when he ut
tered it that he was speaking to the world. His has
been the master mind that for years past has directed
British expansion in South Africa, and now in his
speech there arc revealed to us the thoughts of that
mind and the ideals to which it is devoted.
There is nothing in the speech of true grandeur of
any kind, not even the grandeur of imperialism. The
chief point of exultation of Rhodes is that the year's
profits of the De Beers Company amount to 2,000,000
pounds sterling, and that the company owns 'all the
diamonds wherever its charter exists. After dwelling
with pride upon the wealth of the company in money
and" in' jewels, he spoke of the strenuous defense J of
Kimberley by, its citizens and said: "We have-done
our duty in preserving and protecting the greatest
commercial association in the worldâ her Majesty's
flag."" : "^
Had an enemy said that of the banner of Great
Britain it would have been everywhere regarded as an
unfair slur upSn a standard that upon many; a' hard
fought field has been the beacon of some of the
noblest of national ideals. What shall we think, then,
of such a declaration made, not by a foreign satirist,
but by one of the foremost Englishmen of his time?
In the mind of Cecil Rhodes her Majesty's flag is
but the symbol of: a commercial organization.. .It
stands for nothing but trade and such conquest ; as
trade demands for its profits. It is not to advance
civilization, nor to take up the white man's' burden
of educating the barbarian, but solely to promote the
interests of merchants and manufacturers, and es
pecially of the De Beers Company, that sq many
brave British youths and gallant gentlemen have gone
to their death in South Africa. To such a degree of
abasement under the rule of Chamberlain and Rhodes
has come her Majesty's flag,* and her nobles â¢ and
her peasants are .made to do the service of stock
jobbers and gold speculators as they follow to death
what was of old proudly proclaimed:
"The â¢ flag that braved a thousand years
The battle and the breeze.". 1
An ex-convict who has served the State in stripes
for bribing juries resented his ejection the other day
from a local courtroom. He insisted that his word
is as good as that of anybody else. While he is cer
tainly entitled to his opinion there is an; emphatic
objection to his exercise of his judgment in -our
courts. - .

PINHEAD MANAGEMENT.
THE patrons of the Sunset Telephone and Tele
graph Company in Berkeley,' Alameda" and
Oakland are just now being subjected to loss,
inconvenience and. vexation as the result of pin
head management of the affairs of that corporation.
It undertook to provide telephone facilities for a
population of about 100,000. It put up lines, using
private buildings, ornamental trees and other things
not belonging to "h to i string (and stretch its wires.
It then* provided a numbered system for the 'phones,
with certain prefixes, as "blue," "red," "black,"
"main," and so: on, for identification of the lines.
After this system has been long in operation, people
have become accustomed to it, and it has the con
venience that comes from .permanence, and habit, and
after hundreds of business men have. their cards and
stationery printed .with their telephone number and
its identifying prefix, "suddenly, and without notice,
the whole prefix system: is radically changed, substi
tuting prefixes that have no meaning, are difficult .to
remember and have'no element of fitness Â» or con
venience. As a result thousands of dollars' worth of
"cards and stationery,' business and private, are made
useless, wasted and lost, and the use of telephones is
made complex, vexatious and annoying.
A corporation which enjoys a' monopoly, exacts a
high rent for- the use^of its convenience, and under
takes to. supply one ;thafo has become a necessity oi
individual and commercial -life, can justify its
monopoly only by intelligent 'management and a
policy that is at one with the interests of its patrons.
All these principles are violated by the pinhead man
agement of the Sunset. Knowing in advance the ter
ritory it had to cover, the. radiation of its principal
lines, their length and all other facts concerning the
system, it would have required only some brains and
some respect for the rights of the public to have,
inspped out its system of designations, and arranged
its prefixes, once for all, so that no change would
have been needed. Then when its patrons were ac
customed to the arrangement all might have enjoyed
the use of the telephones, with no more than the un
avoidable inconvenience of waiting for the disengage
ment of a line, or the avoidable vexation caused by
the exhaustion of the overworked and underpaid girls
in the central office.
The corporation has chosen to add the avoidable
to the unavoidable exasperation incident to the use
of its wires and instruments, and on top of this has
piled a very large loss involved in obsoleting the pre
fixes on the printed stationery of its customers. Thi3
means also a current loss of business. Patrons who
order by telephone are for a time led astray, cannot
find the store they want to reach by wire, and ..in im
patience and haste get to any one they can reach; 7:
This burden, added to nearly similar erratic. changes
in the running of street cars in Oakland "and Ala
meda, adds an inconvenience to life and business on
that side of the bay which is seriously reflected in
harm to large interests, to progress, to access of popu
lation, and to all those things which make for the
prosperity of a community.
As we have said, it'is done without notice. Had
the corporation been mindful of anything but the col
lection of its own rents and nickels it would have
given a notice of intended change long enough iii' ad
vance to have permitted the use of business and pri
vate stationery already "printed, so that there would
have been no loss. This would have somewhat
softened the idiocy of the management in its conse
quences to the victims. ..But there is no such disposi
tion nor forethought shown, Â¦ and monopoly presents
itself in the most swinish form; regardless of the harm
dene and the inconvenience inflicted. .â¢ -..
; After a great deal of wrangling, misunderstanding
i and hard words. .Germany and the United States
j have at last struck an opinion- of common accord.
Germany doesn't want any of our embalmed beef,
and neither do, we. <

SUPERIOR . WILL POWERâ R. R.,
City.- This correspondent writes: "Miss
A., who >s physically weak, places her
hands-on a small table; Mr. 8., who Is
very strong, also places his hands on the
same table,- and .In a. few moments Miss.
A. says that B. cannot move the table,
and although he tries bis- best to do so
he is unable to move it. Does she hyp
notise him?" That is a case of superior
will power,' and has been classed as mag
netism and hypnotism. - A person pos
sessed of : a superior will power controls
one of weaker power and can induce .the
other to do or not do what the stronger
will may desire.

COMMERCIAL MUSEUM PLANS.
I |ROM the report submitted to the promotion
I committee of the commercial museum by the
â¢ subcommittee on plan of organization good rea
sons may be drawn for sanguine hopes of the success
of the project. The plan suggested for the govern
ment of the museum, when established, is excellent,
and the programme of action outlined for immediate
work promises good results.
According to the suggestions of the report the in
stitution is to be known as. the Pacific Commercial
Museum; its objects arc to gather and to give to its
members information that will assist in developing
commerce with foreign countries, and particularly all
countries in and around the Pacific Ocean; also to
establish a connection with the Philadelphia Com
mercial Museum, and other like institutions, by which
information may be obtained from the rest of the.
world which would be of advantage to the pro
ducers, manufacturers and merchants who may, be
members of the institution.
For immediate work it is recommended there shall
as soon as possible be appointed a finance committee
of sixty prominent citizens, to which all the members
of the promotion committee shall be added. It is \he
duty of this committee to make a thorough 'canvas's of
the States of the Pacific Coast, and receive donations
and subscriptions from all steamship companies, rail
roads, banks and other corporations, as well as from
capitalists, merchants, manufacturers, real estate"own
ers and all 'others interested in the development of
our commerce. The committee is furthermore to
canvass for members of the museum and to collect
from each subscriber his initiation fee.
There will be of course differences of opinion as
to the details of the plan of administration, but those
are minor matters and can be left for adjustment
hereafter. The, main object now is to get the mu
seum established. It is not a small undertaking, ai:d
a good deal of money will. have to-be forthcoming.,
Fortunately the importance of. the issue is well un
derstood by the progressive men of the coast, and an
elaborate preliminary campaign of education ,\vill
hardly be necessary. The enterprise appeals to the
personal interests as well as to the public spirit of
the merchants, manufacturers and producers of the
country', and it is reasonable to expect them to give it
a prompt and earnest support.

BLOW AGAINST
BROTHERHOOD
ASSOCIATIONS
New Southern Pacific Relief
Department Opposed. by -
Employes.
They Claim That It Is an Indirect
Means to Weaken Their Well-
Established Protective
Societies.
Over at the Oakland mole, where the
trainmen and the engineers congregate,
there may be seen at any hour of the day
groups of Southern Pacittc employes gath
ered around a big white chart hung con
spicuously in the boxroom. It is the new
prospectus of the Southern Pacific relief
department, and trainmen, brakemen, en
gineers, conductors and mechanics are up
in arms against the new ...order of the
company." They claim that It is a direct
blow at their brotherhood associations and
allows them no cnance but to take out
policies in the new venture, of the com
panyâthe insurance business.
The relief department plan provides for
sick benertts as well as insurance money.
This Is where the shoe pmenes painfully,
for the members of the brothernoods re
ceive sick benefits from their own organi
zations. In fact, one of the reasons for
the existence of the brotherhoods is the
sicK beneht assurance. At present all or
the majority oi the men employed by the
{southern .facinc on me trains or. in the
yarus belong to one or other of the broth
ernood associations. They claim tnat they
cannot ' stand the doubie expense that
would result if they were members of
botn organizations and that the company.
Knowing this, has inaugurated a relief de
partment to weaken and ultimately 0.6
jstroy tne brotherhoods. In many in
stances," it Is said, the pressure ot dues
would be too great to bear, but the train
men are wary of orienumg the company,
fearing displacement If they fall to come
m witn the tresh tide ol members who
will soon join the relief department.
"Where will our brothernoods be?" is
the cry among the railroad laborers.
After a lengthy introduction, which re
cites the charitable inclination and atti
tude of the company toward its employes,
the- framers of the circular reach tne
business . part of the programme. . The
way the Southern I'aculc Company pre
sents its gilded package is to disclaim
any. lntent to force employes into me re
lict department. Membership on the part
of all employes in the service of the com
pany prior to March 1,-1900, will be volun
tary and without regard to physical con
dition or age, but tney must enter tne
class required by their, salary. A limited
time in which to become members under
the above conditions is allowed, and this
limit is fixed at one year. After the ex
piration of that time any employe who
wishes to join the department must sub
mit to a physical examination and must
not be over 45 years of age. All persons
who enter the service of the company
after March 1 are required to become
members of the relief department.
According to, the scheme, classes are
formed based on the salary of the em
ploye. The charges for membership, the
sick benefits paid and the insurance In
case of death, depend for their amount on
this classification.- But there are several
reasons why the employes of the company
are hotly antagonistic to the foisting of
this new relief scheme upon them. Be
cause of the section which forces all new
employes to join the department and the
other, .which allow* but a year in which
to take out a membership, the servants of
the railroad .corporation believe that they
will be. forced either Into the scheme or
they must go glimmering down the road
of the unemployed. Â¦â¢ Â¦: ?\ Â¦ Â¦ Â« . â¢
Another section In the application for
membership which fills the trainmen with
alarm is a release clause. In case of death
by accident the surviving relatives receive
the insurance money, but the applicant
for membership on joining signs a release
to the company absolving it from any lia
bility for damages. Â¦
ANOTHER "HOLD-UP" EXPOSED.
\A AYOR PHELAX, in his eagerness to com
/ \ promise the newspapers which have criticized
..Â¦:*-, the misfeasance and malfeasance of his ad
ministration, asked some brash questions of J. B
Crockett, president of the San Francisco Gas and
Electric Company, at the hearing before the Board
of Supervisors on Monday, and thereby brought
ibont the exposure of another "hold-up" on the part
of his pet journal, the Examiner. The incident was
amusing in itself, but was made more so by the
ivriggle with which the Examiner endeavored yester
day morning to slip out of the record and leave the
rhxrcf at the door of The Call.
This is the way the examination was reported in
the yellow faker:
President Crockett was put on the stand, and he
6T7ore that ois:trs' salaries of the company amount
to $12,601 a year.
"Xow, let us come to the advertising expenses
of your company," said the Mayor. "Is it not a
fsct that you weTe stood up by a newspaper for
th' sum of $5000 for a single advertisement?"
â¢Yes." said the president, "but we paid $2500
only, for a World's Fair edition."
"Did you get value receivedP"
"We did not."
"Wnat have your ads cost you this yearP"
"Not to exceed 5500."
"How do you account for the fact that you have
not been molested.?"
"I don't know."
"Was it the Chronicle that wanted $2500 P"
"It was not."
"Was it The Call?"
"I don't know that it makes any difference in
this investigation."
"Then you decline to answtr because. I named
the right piper?"
"You have no right to assume that." â¢
The inquiry was then dropped.
The closing assertion, "The inquiry was then
dropped," contains a suggested lie. It is true tht
Mayor made no further inquiries, but it is not true
that the subject was dropped. Mr. Crockett had
something to say. After bringing out the evidence
thai a demand for $5000 was once made upon the
gas company by a newspaper of this city, the ques
tions and answers proceeded thus:
"Did the newspaper get the money?" asked the
Mayor, eagerly.
"T"_:e paper did not get it," replied Crockett,
"but it did get $2500."
"What papsr was thatP" asked the Mayor.
"Oh, I don't care to say," was the laughing re
ply cf Crockett. "I don't want to get them all
down on me."
"Was.it the ChronicleP" asked the Mayor.
: "It was not," was the reply.
"Y~as it The CallP" the Mayor asked, and be
fore Crockett could reply, added: "I see you hesi
tate. Since you stop there I assume that it was
The Call." BESS . -
"You have no right to assume anything of the
sort,*' was Crockett's angry retort. "If you con
tinu? to ask me the names of all the newspapers
you will finally get your answer. We paid that
$2500 to a newspaper which issued a World's Fair
edition."
It 'was after the statement of Mr. Crockett that the
*T.dM-up" had been committed by the paper that is
Â£i;cd "a World's Fair edition" that the inquiry was
e'rojr, cd. It was dropped from the mouth of the
Mayor at that point like a hot potato, for the Mayor
perceived when too late that he had exposed another
of the rascalities of his pet paper.
I: is the Examiner that issued the notorious
World's Fair edition, as is well known to all San Fran
cisco. It is that yellow journal which used the edition
as a means oi getting itself on the payroll of every
corporation in sight, pulling down the Southern Pa
cific to the tune of $30,000, and demanding $5000 of
the gas company. It is that journal which has ever
since been howling because the gas company paid but
half the amount demanded, and the railroad cut off
its subsidy before the full' s3o,ooo -was; paid up.
Such are the facts of the case. Is anybody sur
prised that the Mayor dropped the inquiry?
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, FEBRUARY 28, 1900,
LICENSING CRIME FOR COIN.
AN afternoon paper yesterday announced that a
., majority of the Board of Supervisors are in
â¢ favor of granting a license for a renewal of
racetrack gambling. It said: "If the opinions ex
pressed by individual- members of the Board of Su
pervisors count for anything, no member of the
sporting fraternity need pass a single sleepless night
pondering over the possibilities of the reopening ot
Ingleside. It is as certain as the rising of the sun, or
the going down of the same."
It then added that the Supervisors demand of the
gamblers the payment of a license tax amounting to
$100 a day to the city treasury, the argument being
that the sum is needed on account of the deficiency in
the municipal finances. One gentleman is quoted as
saying, **\Ve are almost compelled to do evil that good
may come of it," and it is asserted that the same view
is taken by others.
. On that showing the people of San Francisco will
have something to say concerning both the policy and
the principle of the proposed action. The gamblers
can well afford to pay $100 a day for the privilege of
running their nefarious trade at Ingleside, for their ill
gotten profits would be enormous; but what benefit
would that amount of money be to the city? Forty
days' racing at $100 a day would give a revenue of
only $4000. How far will that sum go in making up
the deficiency in the treasury? For how long a time
could the streets be lighted with that amount of
money?
If we are to license gambling for the purpose of
raising a municipal revenue, we shall have to enter
upon it in a wholesale way. Why should the Super
visors restrict their favors to Eastern gamblers, or
those at the racetracks, when more money could be
obtained by licensing those downtown? There are
many men who will pay $100 a day for permission to
run a roulette game. There are others who will pay
that amount for permit for a faro game. Licensed
poolrooms downtown would bring in many an addi
tional $100 a day. A pool-selling room for women ex
clusively would bring in another $100. If we are to
permit crime for coin, it will be a poor policy to limit
the license to the Eastern sports and deny it to the
local gamblers.
The principle of the issue is not one to be argued
about. It is vicious and villainous under every aspect. A
community which for the sake of providing itself with
a revenue deliberately licenses a trade that corrupts
men, degrades women, ruins youth, wrecks families
and directly encourages crime of every degree, from
petty theft to murder and suicide, is unfit to be eri
ti listed with self-government. If this license be
granted it will be the blackest blot that could ever dis
grace the record of the city, and bitterly and griev
ously would many a family pay for it.
In Berkeley when some of the municipal authori
ties hesitated to close saloons because they wished the
revenues which are derived from saloon licenses,
seme of the citizens promptly guaranteed the money
in order that the saloons might be closed. If San
Francisco were in such dire need of $4000 as to afford
even a shadow of excuse for licensing the iniquity of
racetrack gambling, the merchants would come forth
here, as in Berkeley, to guarantee the fund rather
than submit the city to the dishonor of such a policy.
" Finally it is to be noted that the plan is as futile as
it is wicked. If forty days' gambling be granted for
$4000, there will be as a result of that moral degrada
tion such an increase of crime that more than $4000
will have to be expended for the trial and conviction
of the guilty. We shall, in fact, bring suffering upon
the innocent, temptation to the weak, and encourage
the vicious to crime, for less than nothing. There can
be no profit in licensing gambling. The toleration of
crime for the sake of coin can end in nothing but a
curse to all.
Let the Supervisors examine the records of the
Police Courts and the Morgue, and compare those of
the days when track gambling was permitted at Inglc
side with those fince, -and see what track gambling
means. It is a subject they should study in order to
learn what it is they are asked to license.

BY. THE REV. E. E. HALE. D. D
The most careless reader -of the Bible
knows that the Psalms were the hymns of
the Hebrews. Even our English name Im
plies that. But. as our Bible* are gen
erally printed, the reader does not know
outside the book of Psalms, whether what
he reads In the English version were writ
ten as poetry Is written, with the enthu
siasm and purpose of poetry, or whether
he Is to read it as he reads the hard facts
of history. ">> .: ; ,f
The mott important contribution, per
haps, mado by the revised version to the
popular reading of the Bible Is the ar
rangement in the printed page of the po
etry, in each Testament co that to every
reader's eye it looks like poetry. Every
reader sees that it is poetryâ that it was
written by some poet whose work was so
good that it has survived the millenniums
of time. Every one knows that he is to
read it as he reads poetry. Kear the end
of the book of Deuteronomy, for instance,
the blessing of Moses, before he diedâ
printed as a poemâ reads;
Tho Lord came from Sinai. .
And rose from Seir unto them;
He ahined forth from Mount I'aran
And He came with ten thousands of holy ones.
And His right hand was a nery law unto them.
And so the ode or song of triumph goes
on with a reference to each of tne twelve
tribes. The mere form or the lines sug
gests poetry, where the old prosaic text
ÃBtd by the early English translators sug
gests the patient narrative of an annalist:
And he said, the Lord came from Sinai, and
rose up from Selr unto them; He shlned forth
from Mount Paran. and He came with ten
thousand of saints; from Ilia right hand went
a nery law unto them.
The commission which prepared our new
or revised version says of the prophets
tnat they have not extended to the pro
phetical books this arrangement In lines,
oecause the language of these books "la
rather of the nature of lofty and Impas
sioned prose, except In purely lyrical pas
rages." Lofty and Impassioned prose
comes very near to poetry. And in gen
eral the prophetical books of the Old tes
tament could be ranked as poetical. This
means everything from the Prophet Isaiah
to the end of the Old Testament.
Speaking roughly, we may say that the
first half of the Old Testament, the books
which go as far as Esther in the usual ar
rangement, are narrative or historical;
that after that point, beginning with the
book of Job, they are poetical. The book
of Job is often called a dramatic poem.
The Psalms are a collection of several
odes and hymns, from many authors In
many centuries. Then follow the Pro
verbs, which are cast in poetical form; the
book of Ecclesiastes, which, while printed
as prose in the revised version, is fre
quently marked with the characteristics
of Hebrew poetry; The' Song of Songs,
which has all the characteristics of lyrlo
poetry, and then the prophets.
In the first or historical part are many
songs or odes. Such is that which has
been cited of Moses: such Is the song of
Deborah. In the prophetic books, on the
othtr hand, we have sometimes a frag
ment of history. But, speaking rather
roughly, as I said, the first half may be
called historical and the second poetical.
Rhythm, Meter and Form.
It is impossible In a single paper like
this to go Into any detail as to the dis
tinctions between the external form of
Hebrew poetry and that to which we are
accustomed. It must be enough to say
that it does not seem to have had any
rhyme, and that the rhythm of the versos
is not to be strictly marked by the court
ing of syllables. We know too little of the
original pronunciation of the words to say
whether what we call "quantity" or what
we call "accent" was considered In the
composition.
One peculiarity Is observed In which t^e
Hebrew poets Indulged themselves Â»nurh
more frequently than those of the western
nations. It is generally spoken of a*
"parallelism." This word means the mejh
od of composition by which one line or
verse takes up the language or perhaps
the Idea of a previous verse and repeats
it, with more or less variation, in other
words. The writers of Hebrew odÂ«s or
Â£onfrs frequently used this method in
arranging verses which should be sung al
ternately by different choirs. There is a
resemblance to the strophe and the anti
strophe of and Greek choruses, but
the alternate verses in the Hebrew poema
are much shorter than those of the Greek.
Imagine one choir on one side of a tern
pie singing:
The heavens declare the glory of God.
And another on the other side respond
ing:
Ar.d the firmament showeth Hla handiwork.
Chorusâ Day unto day uttereth speech. .
Anti-chorusâ And night unto night showeth
knowledge.
Chorusâ There Is no speech nor language.
Anti-chorusâ Their voice cannot be heard.
Chorusâ Their line is gone out through all
the earth.
Anti-chorus â And their words to the end of
the world.
In America the late Dr. Hastings intro
duced in many churches a renewal of such
alternate singing. Those who study the
subject may well read his little treatises
upon It.
Another feature which occasionally op
pears in Hebrew poetry Is the reference,
literally, to the letter with which the
l>oÂ«-ms are written. For instance, the lL'tth
psalm consists of twenty-two stanza
each of eight lines. In the original the
first verse consists of lines beginning w!tn
the letter A. tho second verse of lines bo
ginning with thei letter B. and In rnoÂ«t
Bibles the Hebrew letter Is printed Â«it the
beginning of these separate subdivisions.
This sort of Ingenuity, sometimes show-
Ing Itself in acrostics, and sometimes In
other ways, appears in many of the
poems.
The Spirit.
But It is the spirit of Hebrew poetry
which has given It its value, an infinite
value to the world. --^-.jjvv.-/
And David's lyre is mightier than his throne.
There were very likely many poem 3 o*
ballads written in the Hebrew language
which have not come down to us which
were not so bathed in the divine spirit as
those which have survived. There is no
reason why lovers should not have writ
ten love songs, why soldiers should not
have written ballads, why meditative in
dividuals should not have written what
used to be called didactic poems, in Pales
tine as well as elsewhere. But for what
ever reason, the" poems which Â¦ survive
are, almost without exception, . poems
alive with the life of God. The writer
looks at the subject v/ith which he has to
deal with distinct reference to the In
finite life which controls movement, which
gives effect to cause, and Is the vigor and
strength of what he Is to fay. I once
wrote a little sketch In which, for. my
purpose, I Imagined a meeting between
Homer and David. I wanted to contrast
the poetry of the Greeks with the poetry
of> the Hebrews. I made Homer uing his
description of a snowstorm, and David, in
comparison, sing to him the 147 th psalm:
He glveth mow like wool:'
He scattersth the hoar-front like aÂ»hfes;
He'caiteth forth His le* like morsels;
Who can Â«tand before His cold?
He sendeth forth His wind and mclteth them:
He causeth His wind to blow and th*. waters

"Always this 'He,'" said one of the younj?
soldiers to another.
"Yes." he replied, "and it was so In the be
rlnning of the evening. *hen we were above
there."
- "There is a strange difference between the
two men. thoujrh the one plays as well as the
other, and thn Greek speaks with quite as little
foreign accent as the Jew, and their subjects
are th< ram*." â¢ , â¢
"Yes." Â»a!d the young Philistine harper; "If
the Greek should sing One of - the Hebrew's
songs you would know he had . borrowed it in
a moment."
"'.'And to If It were the other. way." â¢
"Of course," said tbelr old captain. Joining
In the- conversation. "Homer, If you call him
so, . slnrs the thing made: David sings the

VI .was going to Bay that Homer would sing

GOLDEN AGES OF* LITERATURE.

tftn Life cf lives or the King of kings.
And he Is writing or singing because ha
