life and move nod have our being."
The Psalms.
The great German poet. Herder, mor9
than a hundred years ago wrote some
very valuable essays on Hebrew poetry,
which have a great interest for persons,
who really want to read the Old Testa
ment in the spirit of those who wrote it
and with an understanding of the custom*
of the timÂ« in which it was written aal
the people for whom It was written.
This book, I will say In passing, would
be of great value for people who are con
ducting Sunday classes in church and try
ing to interest young people in the books
of the Old Testament. â¢ Â¦Â¦ â¢
Herder says again and again In the
course of these essays that whoever wants
really to get at the Mfc and movement of
the . poetical books of the Hebrews ha I
better study the book of Psalms ftrjt.
Make that the center- of the whole of the
Old Testament, he says. He says that
the historical psalma. written perhaps
long after the events referred to. are t?ie
Interpreters of the historical books. Fur
Instance, a person understands the narra
tive of the crossing of the Red Sea better
after he has acquainted himself with tpe
triumphant song 3 In which hundreds of
years after the Israelites Thanked God for
his goodness on the occasions there de
scribed.
Their Authors and Aims.
Remember, then, that the book of
Psalms, as we have it, consists of five
different collections of psalms, ma-la at
different times, and probably by different
people. It is quite as in an old church you
sometimes find a hymn book which con
tains the Psalms of David as translated
by Dr. Watts, together with a collection
of hymns made by one minister, and an
other collection of hymns made by ar.
other. Of these five subdivisions the flrjt
ends with Paalm 41. the second wnh
Psalm 72. the third ends with the 89th and
the fourth with the l'>>th Psalm. But the
first two cannot be said to be the o!d<jat.
for some pieces are found among them
which belong to the period of the captiv
ity. The collection of the whole was prob
ably made as late as the second century
before Christ. Every one of these collec
tions has some psalms of David; and tt.o
paalms of David, more than those of ai-y
other writer, give the character to the
book. Names of authors given at the be
ginning of the psalms are not to be con
sidered very important. But In some in
stances which interest us most these
names may be regarded aa the names cf
the real authors. For instance, the 3Ctn
Psalm may be regarded as really by
Moses, though some eminent critics navo
tried to show that It was composed tn tho
period of the captivity.
If now the reader, trylns to acquaint
himself with the Hebrew poetry, will for
himself divide the psalms of David the
shepherd from the psalms written after
ward by David the king, he will begin to
get some idea of what has been meant
when we have spoken of the relation of a
poem to the circumstances of its composi
tion. Take such a psatm as that beauti
ful 133 d, which describes the friendship
between Hermon and Zion. The shepherd
poet Is on some eminence, probably to the
east of the Jordan, where he can see the
clouds gathering above the forests of Her
mon. and can see them pass south over
/he plain of Esdrael and see them dis
till in the rain over the rocks of Zion.
Seeing this, as David the shepherd may
have seen it. he writes the lines which
most of us remember:
It is the Jew of Hermon.
That cometh down upon the mountain of Zion;
For there the Lord commanded the blessing.
Even life for evermore.
On the other hand, after David iÂ» a king,
after he has obtained the mastery over
the tribes which Infested Israel with their
inroads, he will sing a statesman's song:
He maketh wars to ceaso unto the end of the
He breaketh the bow. and cutteth the Â»peae
In sunder;
He burneth the chariot in the fire.
And when he praises God It is not now
because He has "led us as goats over the
mountains." or "strengthened our hands
so that they break an iron bow.' but be
cause God Is a "King over all the ewlh.
It would also be a good exercise care

torical books, where you will find more
than one instance where his poetry is in
troduced in the very place where th* ode
or the poem was composed.
But you may go further than this, sou
may read so carefully in the history on

selves the impression a* to trie Â»lac 3

TO PREVENT TAX
OFFICE ERRORS
The publication of the story In yester
day's Call to the Â«ffect that grave errors
and omissions had occurred in the records
left by former Tax Collector Sheehart has
Impelled Auditor Wells to take steps that
will reduce tbe possibility of sucb errors
happening again.
.The Auditor yesterday sent a communi
cation to the Board of calling
attention to the fact that It has been cus
tomary for the Tax Collector upon receiv
ing money for taxes to receipt the tax
bills with a rubber stamp bearing his
name and that of the cashl*r. The Audi
tor advises that the signing by a rubber
stamp Is an Illegal act and the custom
should be discontinued by a resolution of
the board directing the Collector of Taxes
to receipt all bills In handwriting by hti
cashier or any of his deputies.
MEETING OF CATTLE
MEN WILL BE LARGE
All the States and Territories of the
Pacific Coast will t>e represented at the
meeting of the cattlemen to take place
In this city next Monday. The purpose Is
to take action in favor of the Foster bill
now before Congress providing for leasing;
the' public grazing lands for ten yours,
with the right of renewal at the end of
ten years. Responses have been received
from fifty cattlemen who were invited to
attend and all but two have promised to
be present personally or by representa
tive.

No one was hurt.

ADVEKTISEMEUTS.
SOLD HER PROPERTY JUST
SEVEN HOURS TOO SOON
Mrs. Cockerill's Contract With Real
Estate Agents , Lasted a Little
Longer Than She Thought.
Burnham & Marsh, the real estate
agents, yesterday won a suit in Presiding
Justice Dunn's court against Josephine
Cockerill for the recovery of $171 25 fea real
estate brokers' commission. The evidence
showed that the defendant had executed
a contract on September 25, ISS3. authoriz
ing Burnham & Marsh to sell a piece of
property on Broderlck street, near Cali
fornia, for $6550 for thirty days from the
date of signing. Mrs. Cockerill also
agreed to pay 2^4 por cent commission if
Bhe sold the property hersslf. On October
23 she sent a communication to the real
estate agents reading: "As my contract
expires to-day I will withdraw It."
Judge Dunn hold* that It Is Immaterial
whether the contract was made on Sep
tember 2b. as claimed by the plaintiffs, or
on September 23, according to the defend
ant's testimony, for tho thirty days speci
fied therein would commence to run not
from the time of signing, but from the
date thereof, and the language of the
document of October 23 was evidently
used under a misapprehension as to the
day on which the contract would expire.
The evidence In the case showed that on
October 25, 1893, at about 5 o'clock in the
afternoon, the property was sold by Mrs.
Cockerill for J6"50. The contract had
seven hours of life and the defendant wan
clearly bound by Its terms.
MRS. ROBERT J. BURDETTE.
THE California Club tendered a reception yesterday afternoon to Mrs. Robert
J. Burdette. The affair took place at the headquarters of the club In the
Young Men's Christian Association, and was so well attended that many of
those who had gathered to hear what the bright little lady was going to
say were obliged, to stand in the hall' and catch what they could of the ad
dress as it floated through' the doors.
After a pleasant Introduction -by Mrs. Lovell White, the president of the club,
Mrs. Burdette said, among other things: ; ;. -^
"Club life for women at the present time is an evolution, and one that h.is
not reached its. highest type. Ten years ago it was represented by 'self culture; 1
then came culture ef sel{ through culture of neighbor; then followed,. on a Mill
higher plane, culture through Bervice. We were at one time in danger of pre
venting the saddest of all picturesâ club women whose intellects were being cul
tivated, strengthened, enriched at the expense of the heart. Life cannot lone
continue if head and heart .war against each other.
"Club life, like home life, should be the center, but not the limit of actUlly,
and though I mention club first and home last, 1 by no means so rate them in or
der of importance. My creed in all woman's work is this: That no work, club
work or other interest, should be undertaken that does not emanate from the home,
and in its very best results return to the home, and be always secondary to the
home." . ... .
The beneficent influence of the club upon home life, educational, civic and so
ciety life was touched upon and illustrated. As no club possesses all the virtue
and ability to accomplish this of itself, the benefit of acquiring new methods anu
plans and aims by association with others 'was strongly presented and clearly
set forth. The possibility of doing this through federation led to an elucidation
of the principles of federation and the benefits to the individual clubs and the
individual members.- .
Mrs. Burdctte made a very clear point that no club was to lose its distinctive
individuality in federation, but each-wouid continue to carry on its own work
in its own way as at present..
The gracious and graceful personality of the speaker added much convincing
force to her very cogent arguments. She possesses * a very earnest manner, be
tokening the deepest sincerity, and her voice, which is very winsome and mu
sical adds the highest charm to her ability as a public speaker.
To-day Mrs. Burdette, upon the invitation of Mrs. Phebe Hearst, Mrs. Benja
min Ide Wheeler and Miss Jewett. president of the Associated Women Students,
will address the young women of Berkeley. \
This evening Rev. Robert J. Uurdette, the well-known humorist, who is in the
city with hiÃ wife, will lecture in the Calvary Presbyterian Church for the bene
fit of the Christian Endeavor Society of the First Baptist Church.
The money derived from the entertainment will be devoted to building a new
edifice for the church. â¢ .. â¢ . ....
It is expected that the fame of the lecturer and the good cause for which ho
appears will draw a large crowd, not only of church-going people, but of those
whp like wit and enjoy genuine humor. ,â¢: r'r

.The .mail steamer Australia arrived

After a thorough examination Dr.. Kin
youn allowed the steamer to come to her
dock. . . t .;
M. G. Bale, who came up on the Aus
tralia, had to be taken away in an am
bulance. He is a carpenter and builder,
who went to Honolulu to superintend the
erection of' some Government 1 wharves.
Ho fell from the second story of one of
them and sustained injuries from which
ho nearly died. -He is now on the. road to
recovery, but it will be several months
before he will be able to walk .about
again.
The^Oocldental and Oriental Steamship
Company's Coptic sailed for the Orient
yesterday. She took away an unusually
heavy cargo, 200 Chinese and 25 Japanese
in the steerage. . .
The old whaler Mary fj. Hume has been
turned into a tugboat. The Hume will go
from here to Nome about the end. of
March and on her arrival there will carry
passengers between St. Michacr and the
new gold fields for the Pacific Steam
Whaling Company.

will carry "troops and provisions to and

EASTERN MARKETS.
Chicago Grain Market.
CHICAGO, Feb. 27.â 1t was a quiet day In
the wheat pit, the range the greater part of
the time being Me. at the outside only %c.
The feature was the covering by shorts, local
people leading. Scalpers were on both sides
of the fence working on very small margins.
Liverpool was apparently the only statistical
support, but it was sufficient to Influence those
who had recently sold short. May opened %c
over yesterday at 65y t 65%c, touched 65% c. re
acted to C6'Â»c, eased off to 65%0- and near the

pool cables and the light country offerings.

The oats market was firm In spite of selling

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The area cf high pressure over Nevada and
Utah this morning Is moving eastward. An
area of low pressure has made its appearance
over Northwestern Washington and Vancouver
Island. The storm it not yet well enough de
veio;Â«d to warrant a definite forecast of Its
path. There are come indications, however,
that the Etorm will develop and may cause rain
by Thursday night in Southern California.
Itain will probably fall Wednesday over Cen
tral and Northern California.
Light to heavy frosts occurred this morning
in many places, but no damage was done.
A maximum wind velocity of twenty-six
miles r*r hour from the northwest Is reported
at Yuma.

hourÂ« ending midnight. February 28:

NEW YORK, Feb. 27.â The violent fluctua

allowed by the executors of the estate of

in favor of one of Â¦ the executors and . his
sister. , Other heirs claim that the note, ls
a forgery. .Their petition that the powers
of the executors be suspended pending an
investigation , has been granted by Judge
Gaddis, ,. who to-day appointed John D.
Lawson special administrator. The court
also made the . further.' order, that George
;W. Gregory - and his attorney, W. H.
Grant, , be allowed Â¦ fifteen days within
POSTITM CEREAL.
THE DOCTOR SAID
years old, was struck and Instantly killed
at noon to-day by the eastbound Hay
wards local train. .... r
For miles in either direction from the
point where the aged woman was mangled
there Is a straight track with a clear and
entirely unobstructed view. The day was
clear and there was no reason why Mrs.
Lewis should not have been seen on the
tracks by the trainmen several blocks
away. She was killed at Wheeler avenue,
a much used crossing for residents of the
Fruitvale district. Mrs. Lewis had fre
quently traveled that route in calling
upon a son's family who live on Fremont
avenue, near East Fourteenth street. She
was bound thither to-day when she was
Btruck.
Mrs. Lewis had resided since last July
with her. aged husband, William A. Lewis,
at the home of Urban Lewis, a son, on
Wheeler avenue, three blocks south. of the
railroad tracks; Her comings and goings
were limited principally to visits with her
other son, Charles.
Ten minutes after she left the house to
day her body lay on the railroad embank
ment. It was hurled twenty feet, and
besides the fatal fracture of the spinal
column both shoulders, the left arm and
all of the ribs on the left side were
broken. ;Â» \<-
The train was moving very rapidly
when . It struck the woman. There was
apparently no warning signal given in
time to allow her to escape. The only
witness close to the scene of the accident
outside of the trainmen was J. E. George
of Fruitvale. who lives near the tracks.
He had no direct knowledge, having been
attracted to the tracks by the sharp whis
tling from the <mglne and the sudden
stopping of the train. He was not sure
whether Mrs. Lewis , had started - across
the tracks before the train left Fruitvale
station or after It was very close to her.
Mrs. Lewis' relatives Insist that she had
good eyesight and hearing and had always
been cautious ln crossing the tracks. ,
Mr. and Mrs. Lewis formerly resided In
Los Angeles. They had considerable
property. The children are: Mrs. A M
Bealo of The Needles, Walter S. Lewis
Urban Lewis and Charles Lewis. The
Coroner will hold an inquest probably to
morrow. .
CRASHED INTO A WINDOW
AND DIED OF FRIGHT
Peculiar Cause of an Aged Woman's
% Sudden Death at Emery
ville.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1118 Broadway, Feb. 27.
While groping about in < the darkness
last midnight for the door of her room-
Mrs. Josephine Revel crashed into a
window and the nervous shock produced
heart failure that resulted ln her death
several hours later. Â¦Â¦ â¢
Mrs. Revel resided with her son and
daughter, E. Revel and Mrs. Selvllle Del
sol, on Ninth street, between Folsom and
Powell, Emeryville. She Is a widowed
French woman, 75 years old.
About 1 o'clock this nu^nlns her eon
was suddenly arousad- by the crash of
breaking glass. Mr. Revel hurried to
his mother's room, whence the
noise emanated, ' and â¢ there -he
found her lying prostrate on the â¢ floor.
She revived sufficiently to explain that
she had left her bed and was trying to
find the door when she accidentally ran
against the window. The aged woman
was slightly cut about the forehead, but
othewise her injuries seemed trivial.
The eon helped his mother back to her
couch, but shortly afterward she ap
peared so greatly distressed that Dr.
Wood was summoned. Before the doctor
had arrived Mrs. Revel had breathed her
last.
Coroner Mehrmann and a Jury Inquired
into the death to-night and the verdict
was ln accordance with the facts.

MIRACULOUS
ESCAPE OF A
DIMINUTIVE BOY
John Souza Is Struck and
Pinned Down by Freight
Train Locomotive.
The Wheels Cut Off His Left Shoe
Sole Entirely and His
Big Left Toe Is
Gone.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
1118 Broadway, Feb. 27.
Struck down by a rapidly moving
freight locomotive, little twelve-year-old
John Souza this afternoon experienced
one of the most miraculous escapes from
death on record. '
The boy is the son of Frank Souza, an
employe at the Judson Iron Works, re
siding at 220 Allen street. He had also
been employed at the Iron works near
Kmeryvllle as a nail picker until yester
day, when he was dismissed. But, boy
like, he wandered about the works visit
ing his familiar haunts.
About 4 o'clock this afternoon he was
walking along the railroad tracks oppo
site the' iron works, apparently In deep
meditation, when suddenly he heard the
rumbling of an approaching train. Be
lieving that the train was on the tracks
nearest to him, he walked over to the op
posite tracks. Just as he placed his - loft
foot upon the rail the locomotive drawing
a heavy freight train struck him. He
fell In such a position that his left foot
was pinned under one of the front wheels
of the engine. The wheel cut off the sole
of his shoe clean, and it was supposed
that his foot had been crushed so badly
that . Its amputation was a presupposed
necessity, j. . 'Â¦Â¦-Â¦^ ,-
At the Receiving Hospital, however, it
was found that his only injury consisted
of shock and the loss of the fleshy lower
portion of his big left toe.
"I thought I was surely killed," he said
to Steward Borchert. "I heard no bell,
and am sure the engineer did not blow the
whistle. I heard the train, but thought It
was on the track I was walking by.
PRISONER WAS THOUGHT
TO BE MURDERER DUNHAM
SALINAS. Feb. 27.â Considerable ex
citement was caused ln this city this
evening by the report that a man in Jail,
charged with horse stealing, was no other
than Dunham, the Campbell murderer.
The man stole the horse two weeks ago
and' was afterward captured ln Hanford.
He gives his name as Ashton. but many
people who have seen him assert he looks
very much like Dunham. Late to-night,
however, the whole story was exploded
by a man who knew the murderer well,
asserting that the prisoner la at least ten
years too young to be Dunham.

DIRECTORS ARE CHOSEN.
Mechanics' Institute Elects Five New
Men and Two Old Ones.
The Mechanics' Institute held Its regu
lar election last night for the purpose of
deciding who should constitute the board
of directors for the year commencing
March *}. The board consists of four
teen members, but of these seven were
holdovers, whose terms of office had not
yet expired, leaving seven to be voted in
at the meeting last night.
There were ten candidates, of whom the
following were elected: George Beanston,
James K." Cosgrove, Samuel C. Irving,
Lewis R. Mead, Rudolph Tausslg, P. J.
Healy and George H. Wallls. Healy and
Wallls were members of the old board
and were re-elected to fill the positions
they held during the last year.
The defeated candidates were three ln
number, William Curlett. lienry L. Tick
ner and Richard Valentine.
The holdovers,, who, together with the
newly elected men, will form the board
are Ernest A. DenJcke, Walter N. Brunt
Rodney Kendrick, John McLaren, Charles
M. Plum; Robert W. Neal and Horace
Wilson. â¢ Â¦ Â¦- â¢. -
There Is some speculation as to the ef
fect the new blood in 1 the board will have
on the next for president, as It
is thought that a combination may be
formed to oust Denicke and place Horace
Wilson in the president's chair.

TALE OF FABULOUS
DEPOSITS OF GOLD
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA,- Wash., Feb. 27.â A wonderful
story Â¦Â¦" of richness comes from Grand
Forks, Klondike. The story Is of such a
sensational character that the Yukon Sun
says ; that under . ordinary circumstances
It would be given no credence. Yet that
paper states that on acclaim within five
miles of Grand Forks workmen recently
tapped a deposit of gravel so rich in gold
as to fairly startle them. Pans running
up into ,.. the hundreds of dollars were
quite common, and for a depth of two
feetâ one foot of gravel and one of bed
rockâit la asserted that $200 per pan was
averaged. The foreman of this work Is
authority for the statement that a clean
up of one day of nine hours, when eleven
men. were employed,- yielded nearly:s2o,
000, and that another, day's work of
thirteen 'men for ten hours resulted in a
yield of over $25,000. At this rate the win
ter's yield would reach a million dollars.

SQUANDERED MONEY
IN THE POOLROOMS
James Cooney Wants His Son Patrick
Arrested for Grand Lar
ceny.
James Cooney, 427 Fair Oaks street,
ewore to a complaint in Judge Conlan's
court yesterday for the arrest of his son,
Patrick, on a charge of grand larceny.
Patrick Is accused of stealing a gold
watch valued at $100 from his father last
Sunday and pawning it.
The boy is another victim of the race
track and has spent all . the money he
could lay his hands on to squander in
poolrooms. When he could not get more
money from his parents he stole the gol-.l
watch and the money he was advanced on
it noon went Into the pockets of the po\>l-
Beelers. As he seemed to be Incorrigible,
his father reluctantly decided upon having
him arrested on the charge of grand lar
ceny, and accordingly swore" to the war
rant yesterday. Detectives Fitzgerald
and Graham were detailed by Captain
Glllen to bring Cooney in when found.
Toomy's Death an Accident.
A Coroner's jury yesterday found that
William H. Toomy, the young man who
was found dead in bed in his room In the
Hancock House at 781 - Mission street
came to his death by accident The father
of Toomy, who was at one time United
States Consul at Munich, Germany, tes
tified that his ' son was addicted to the
use of absinthe and bromide of potassium
The Jury came to the Conclusion that the
unfortunate man went to his room in
toxicated and accidentally turned on the
gas after having extinguished the flame.
Rabbi Myers Wins a Case.
Justice of the Peace Groezinger- yester
day rendered a decision in favor of Rabbi
Isidor Myers and his wife, who were de
fendants in a suit brought by Mrs R.
Jacobson for the return of $255 expended by
Mrs. Jacobson for transportation of her
self and daughter from Melbourne Aus
tralia. The case was bitterly contested on
both sides and occasioned several quar
rels, notably one between Dr. G. I Dan
zlger, who was a witness, and Otto Wise
attorney for the rabbi. Justice Groe
zlngcr held that no evidence had been ad
duced to show Rabbi Myers or his wife
had ever promised to reimburse Mrs. Ja
cobson or to , provide for her when she
reached this city. He stated that he
thought It was a family matter that
should have been settled out of court.

Died Sitting in a Chair.
Edward Hengeland died suddenly about
5 o'clock yesterday morning: while Bitting:
ln a chair in the saloon at 6 Jackson
street. The deceased was employed as a
porter ln the Columbia House, 68 Jackson
street. He was a native of Norway, 62
years old.
An. autopsy performed. at the Morgue
by Surgeon Leland disclosed the fact that
cirrhosis of . the liver was the cause of
death. %
Had His Arm Amputated.
Thomas Madigan, a brakeman employed
by the Southern Pacific Company, while
coupling cars last night on the track be
tween Fourth and Fifth streets on King,
had his arm badly crushed.
He was taken to the railroad hospital,
where his injuries were found to be so
serious that it was necessary to amputate
the injured limb. He will probably re
cover.
was one of the best known seafaring men
on the coast. He had large shipping in
terests and It was he who with his
steamer, the Kxcelslor.brought down the
first shipment of gold from Klondike. He
was 63 years old and leaves a widow, a.
daughter and two sons. The funeral will
be under Masonic auspices from the
Westminster Presbyterian Church next
Thursday.
and existing, aud the constant fear that
the Intend to reduce rates are
the causes assigned by Mr. Crockett. He
says that the stocks of the corporation
are held not by. rich men, but by com
paratively poor people and small inves
tors. This did not seem to harmonize
yer J w iU with his admission that Peter
J. Donahue owns 8000 shares. Mr. Crockett
has a very poor opinion of Supervisors
and said that their frequent election un
der changing conditions and with different
plans of action creates a great deal of
unrest and uncertainty. Attorney Thomas
B. Bishop, who is very carefully watching
the Interests of the San Francisco Gas
and Electric Company at this inquiry,
blinked and nodded in solemn accord to
this assertion of Mr. Crockett. A critical
observer might have noticed that Mr.
Bishop even fluttered as If he were skat
ing on thin Ice. ,
But notwithstanding this dlifrression Mr.
Crockett was progressing famously with
the Mayor, after the tussle with Miller,
when the president of the San Francisco
Gas and Electric Company took a false
step and Irretrievably Injured his Honor's
feelings.
Gas Rebates to Laundrymen,
It was during an inquiry ln reference to
rebates in charges to certain customers
of the corporation. Mr. Crockett admit
ted, ln answer to a question, that the
company does allow a rebate, not exceed
ing 10 per cent of the charge, to certain
customers, "to laundrymen. for exam
ple," he said, "to large laundrymen."
And Mr. Crockett smiled. Ever since
that little affair ln the Police Commission
his Honor the Mayor shies at laundry
men, particularly large ones in large
places. "I wanted to know," he said, "for
a very Important reason. The charter
says that the city shall not pay for gas
more than the minimum price to other
customers. I would like to know Just
what price your most ravored customers
pay."
It was the Mayor's turn to smile. He
was determined that Blggy's shadow
couldn't frighten him even ln a gas In
vestigation. His question happened to be
an important one, bo important that
Crockett refused to answer it. He was
willing to be confidential, but not to that
extent. He promised, on being excused,
that he may be able to-day to show the
city a way out of the four months of
darkness.
Gas Inspector Tupper was the next wit
ness on the stand, and swore that his offi
cial tests' proved that the candle power of
the gas supplied by the San Francisco
Gas and Electric Company averages only
15.94 candles Instead of 23. as the com
pany's experts insist. Mr. Tupper became
seriously tangled with himself, though,
under the cross-examination of Crockett.
The inspector did not even know the
name of the photometer he uses, inno
cently answering that the machine
doesn't possess a name. He retired ln
comparatively good order, however, un
der the guns of the Mayor.
A Body Blow at Gas Stocks.
Charles Ackerman, president of the
Equitable Gas Company, was the next
witness. He was persuasive, friendly, vol
uble and enthusiastic. With a burst of
confidential frankness and an expressed
desire to give a candid, honest opinion,
he assured the Mayor that gas stocks are
the most unsafe Investment ln San Fran
cisco. They are absolutely Insecure, he
said, and he is thoroughly convinced as a
truthful, honest man, that the stock of
the San Francisco Gas and Electric Com
pany is not worth $10 a share. The shares
are utterly unprotected, subject to Super
visorial caprice and the stress of compe
tition. But Mr. Ackerman says his com
pany Is first class and in a short time will
be able to supply gas of excellent quality
for $1 a thousand cubic feet. His gas
works now have a daily capacity of 1,500,
000 feet on an investment of $250,000, exclu
sive of land purchases.
This Is due very largely, Mr. Ackerman
said, to the fact that the expenses of his
company are not nearly as large as those
of the San Francisco Gas and Electric
Company. For example, Mr. Ackerman
gives his legal advice to his company for
nothing, while the San Francisco Gas and
Electric Company pays $15,000 a year for
similar services. Mr. Bishop mildly re
buked the witness and, apologetically tell
ing him that he was mistaken, kindly re
frained from drawing any comparison be
tween the legal lights shod on the two
corporations.
The long inquiry into gas rates was
drawing to a close. The last witness was
L. V. Lowe, a very coy gentleman, placed
upon the stand by the Mayor. Mr. Lowe
admitted that he had said that he would
be called by the San Francisco Gas and
Electric Company. He was asked what
it would cost to duplicate the system of
the San Francisco company and he an
swered $10,00i3,000.
"Did you not offer once," asked the
Mayor, "to duplicate. it for $3,500,000?" -
"I don't remember the amount," was
the answer, "but that was a long time ago
and for a different and Inferior process
than that used by the San Francisco Gas
and Electric Company. It would take
twice the amount to duplicate my sys
tem now." Mr. Lowe was hastily excused.
Electric light Muddle.
The board then began the investigation
of electric lighting, but beyond receiving
a mass of technical and not particularly
satisfactory Information, accomplished
very little. The first witness on the sub
ject was F. G. Waterhouse of the San
Francisco Gas and Electric Company. He
gave the board a great deal of informa
tion in relation to the cost of manufac
turing electric light, but nothing upon
which the board could establish a rate.
A. M. Hunt, engineer and general man
ager of the Independent Electric Light
and Power Company, followed, and with
his testimony the board adjourned until
to-day, when the inquiry will be conclud
ed and the rates fixed. Mr. Hunt was
exhaustive in his explanation of the cost
of manufacturing and distributing elec
tric light. He dwelt particularly upon
the difliculty of fixing rates that will be
Just, to the manufacturer and the con
sumer. He suggested a sliding scale that
will take Into consideration the time,
maximum amount of use and distance
of supply. There may be also a practical
method of establishing a flat maximum
rate and allowing competition to do the
rest. He will submit his suggestions to
day.
There was some discussion over the
question whether or not the Supervisors
must fix a rate for electric light under
the charter. It was ended by a decision
to do so, and the San Francisco Gas and
Electric Company will be expected also
to offer suggestions. The Supervisors
will meet at 10 o'clock this morning to re
sume the water inquiry. The gas and
electric lnvestlgatlop will be taken up
at 2 o'clock, and at night it Is expected
that both will be concluded.

Supervisors Nearing the End
of Their Long, Wearisome
Investigation-
The Public Obtains a Glimpse of the
Art of Stock Financiering.
Secrets of the Electric
Field.
The Supervisors devoted many hours
yesterday to the affairs of the gaÃ and
electric light companies. The corpora
tions under investigation did not take
kindly to the Inquiry and more than one
Â¦sharp passage at arms occurred between
the questioners and the questioned. Facts
of general public interest in reference to
the various concerns were established and
to-day the Supervisors will end their In
vestigation and announce the rates that
wUI rule for the fiscal year beginning with
July. .
The Investigation opened yesterday with
Edward C Jones on the stand. Mr.
Jones has been using every endeavor to
persuade the Supervisors that the San
Francisco Gas and Electric Company is
producing gas at the least possible cost
under the conditions that exist here. He
insisted yesterday that the company
should be treated leniently as ft pays
much higher wages than other concerns
in different parts of the country for the
same labor and is forced to pay
more for Its coal. This item of
coal is an extremely important
one, but the agents of the company de
clare that they are forced to pay very
high prices, higher, indeed, this year than,
last on account of the Boer war, that con
venient excuse for everything costly
from diamonds to ashes.
In great detail Mr. Jones related what
he knows of the operations of gas com
panies in other cities and in every in
stance he drew comparisons favorable to
the San Francisco Gas and Electric
Company. When he was asked to be
more explicit, he seemed to feel hurt and
replied that he was telling all he knew.
Then Supervisor Brandenstein suggested
that if the board did not receive more de
tailed Information it might be forced to
reduce the rate to Jl 25 a thousand cubic
feet. With that ominous suggestion
ringing In his ears Mr. Jones left the wit
ness stand for the noon recess. When
the board met again the representatives
of the San Francisco Gas and Electric
Company were in a savage mood.
The Mayor as a Landlord.
C. O. G. Miller, secretary and treasurer
of the Pacific Gas Improvement Company,
was the first witness called after recess.
He wsjs an extremely unwilling witness,
maintained an air of personal grievance,
refused positively to oe candid and re
torted sarcastically to the Mayor when
ever an uncomfortable question was
asked. Mayor Phelan sought particularly
to learn the character and value of the
plant Mr. Miller represented and to dis
cover the relations existing between the
Pacific Gas and Improvement Company
and the San Francisco Gas and Â£lectrio
Company.
Mr. Miller would admit nothing 1 more
than that the two corporations had a
business understanding, but J. B. Crock
ett was more frank later in the day and
admitted that the understanding between
the companies was to leave one another
alone and to agree upon rates. During
tiie Inquiry made to determine the value
of the plant of the Pacific Company Miller
tald that it was assesseu for *2,C00,000.
"Then you take that sum as the basis
ct valuation?" asked the Mayor, blandly.
tills Honor has very great respect for the
assessments made by Assessor Dodge.
"I did not say so, was Miller's reply.
"I r--imj>ly mentioned the circumstance as
an incident In the~-case. It would be as
just to accept that assessment value as
the true value of our property as it would
he to accept the assessed value of the
Phelan building as the true value of that
property. The Phelan property, as you
know, is assessed for $3000 or S4OOO a foot,
while you know very well that It Is worth
very much more."
It is such cruel retorts as these which
irritate his Honor; but he continued gal
lantly with the Investigation, not deigning
to reply to the disagreeable Mr. Miller.
The Mayor turned to a discussion of the
Ftockn of the company and elicited some
valuable Information, the property being
listed at the Bond Exchange for one-half
oÂ£ what the company insists should be
the basis of its Interest-bearing income.
Miller refused to give any definite expla
nation of the fact and startling discrep
ancy, and then Mayor Phelan asked:
"The high price of gas stocks is due. Is
It not, to the earning capacity of the
stocks, and not to their intrinsic value?"
Shadows on the Phelan Stores.
Miller had been losing his patience very
rapidly. He had no purpose to be candid
and this question snapped from him a
long and sharp answer, in which he dis
played the grief of a personal affront. He
declared that the suggestion conveyed in
the Mayor's question was partly true and
partly false, just in its assertion of a
principle and unjust in its application.
With much bitterness of spirit he 6ald:
"If the Supervisors were to investigate
your Market-street property and allow
you $00 a month rent for your stores I
think the value of your property would
very seriously be affected. It is a very
large question and I don't, pretend to be
an expert regarding It." The Mayor
f-miled blandly and Mr. Miller was ex
cused, much to his evident relief.
J. B. Crockett was next placed on the
Ftand and gave an outline of what he be
lieves to be the causes for the depression
in gas stocks. Competition, threatened

"Stop Coffee and Use Postum Cereal
Coffee."
It seemed a hard matter to jjet
through breakfast without a cup of hot
coffee, so I stuck to it for several
years, although I was a srreat sufferer
from sick headaches, which sometimes
attacked me as often as three times a
week. I used to take medicine for my
head troubles, not knowing the cause of
them, and kept on drinking coffee, until
I finally came down with a serious stom-
ach trouble and had to go to the doctor.
He said I had dyspepsia in a bad form
and some other complications; that I
must stop drinking coffee and tea for a
year or more. I got hold of the Postum
Food Coffee, and since using it have
been entirely free from the headaches
and stomach trouble.
"It has evidently been a great benefit
for me, for my health has been revived
and I have grown fleshy since begin-
ning its use. My husband and daughter
also drink Postum and like it very
much. Daughter does not think she can
have a breakfast without her Postum.
I find people occasionally who have
tried Postum, but complain that it is
not ftrong enough. I find in such cases
that they are cither helter-skelter house-
keepers or have been very careless in
the preparation of the Postum Food
Coffee. It requires full fifteen minutes
of actual boiling after the bubbling be-
gins, and two heaping teaspoonfuls to
the person. This gives an elegant cup
of coffee. Any one who is troubled
with coffee drinking can well afford to
abandon it and take up the Postum
Food Coffee." Mrs. L. W. Bell. 2532
Nicollet aye.. Minneapolis, Minn. .
Some good housekeepers mix the
Postum, four heaping teaspoons to the
pint of water, in a very little water, and
place it in a tall coffee pot so it will not
boil over, then after it has begun to
boil and been stirred down they add
enough water to make up the requisite
amount of liquid. ,

BURNS HANDICAP.
List of Probable Starters for the Bich
- Prize, With Jockeys Who Will
Be Seen in the Saddle.
The great $10,000 Burns handicap, at one
and a quarter miles, to be decided on Sat
urday next. Is now the all-absorbing topic
of conversation among horsemen, not
only in this State but in the Eastern
States as well. Its rich value each year
calls out the best horses in training, until
it is now recognized as one of the classic
events of the American turf. This year
the field promises to be the largest since
the Inception of the stake, for from the
present outlook thirteen and probably six
teen horses will go to the post.
When the horses are summoned the en
semble will be about as follows: .

THE CALL'S RAGING CHART.
Policemen "On the Carpet."
The Police Commissioners met last
night and dismissed the charge of fall-
Ing to aslst a fellow officer preferred
against Patrolman James Norton. Spe
cial Officer Deasy, â¢ charged with assault-
Ing a . citizen named James Wood, with
whom he had trouble, was also acquitted.
Special Officer Dennfs Murphy was laid
off for thirty days for imbibing too freely.
He promised the Commissioners that he
would never drink again. The charge of
failing to properly patrol his beat, made
against Policeman Hemenez, was dis
missed. *QBttM^BM!
The Commissioners will hold another
meeting at 4 o'clock this afternoon, at
which time. It is '. understood, . they will
reorganize the detective department.
Bogan Given the Decision.
MEMPHIS, Feb. 27.â Fred Bogan of
San Francisco was awarded the decision
over Tom Cooney of Boston at the Audi
torium this afternoon in the nineteenth
round. Both men were on their feet and
fighting furiously when a dispute arose
among the timekeepers, resulting in such
a disturbance that the police stopped the
bout. The referee save a decision for
Bogan. Â¦

PLIMBEnS.
OLYMPICS WIN AGAIN
AT INDOOR BASEBALL
Defeat Beliance Athletic Club Team
by One Bun in an Exciting
Contest.
The- Indoor baseball teams representing
Olympic and Reliance clubs met In a
championship contest last night In the
Olympic Club gymnasium. The Olympics
won by one run, after being apparently
hopelessly out of it In the fourth Inning,
when the score was 9 to 4 against them.
They took a brace after this and scored
eight runs to Reliance's, two. The game
was replete with incident, the features
being two home runs by Captain James
of the Olympics and one by Freeman of
the Reliance Club. The teams lined up:
Olympicsâ Stock, left short; Durham,
right field; James, catcher; Morton, sec
ond base; Gimmel. right short; McNevins,
left field; Butz. pitcher; Pray, third base;
Monahan, flrat base.

fred, right short; Freeman, second base;
"Wynnes, first base; "White, center field;
Dieckmann, left short; Rosenhelm.
pitcher; Brockhage, catcher; Agnew, left
held.

SOFALA EARNS
ANOTHER PAIR
OF BRACKETS
Fleet Filly Seems Unbeatable
in the Two- Year-Old
Sorambles.
Three Choices Successful at Oakland.
Mary Kinsella Let the Talent
Fall With a Heal Dull
Thud.
Bookmaker Barney Schrelber's cheerful
running filly Sof ala celebrated her
seventh victory In the two-year-old dash
at Oakland yesterday. She met Count
Hubert,' Aphrodls and three green 'uns in
Modder, Madrina and Spindle, beating
them as if It were a pleasure instead of a
duty. Not much better than 9to 20 could
be had about the fast young miss, which
price seemed acceptable. Count Hubert
outsprinted her the first .part of It, but
could not hold out, falling short' a couple
of length at the wire, while the winner
was just beginning to catch the breeze.
Modder, a Candelmas colt, from the Jen
nings stable, heavily played for the show,
had his colors lowered for that position
by Aphrodls. .
'Louis. Ezell's fllly, Loch Katrine,
slipped Into the opening event under very
favorable conditions -and at odds of 3 to
5 won very easily. Henry landed Glenn
Ann In the place, a length before'Pongo.
"A cheap bunch started in the . third
number, with Romany closing a slight
favorite over Strongoli. Almoner, ridden
by Jenkins, led to within a few yards of
the wire, where Ruiz brought Strongoli
up, winning by a neck. Croker lost the
show to TallÃC by a head.
One of the most costly upsets experi
enced by the talent was the overthrow of
the 9 to 20 favorite Mary Kinsella by Rio
Chico in the mile and a furlong run. Billy
Magrane's mare, with Bullman up, was
looked upon as a moral and when Rio
Chico. went out and led from start to
finish it was a hard morsel to digest.
There were but four starters, with None
Such taking the show from Tewanda.
John Bullman did not greatly distin
guish himself by his ride on Mortgage in
the fifth number, a six-furlong sprint.
The mare went to the post a slight fa
vorite over Vesuvian, with the educated
coin all on the latter. After a long delay
Spencer on Vesuvian got a flying start
and without effort led out Bessie Lee a
length. Mortgage blew In from the far
rear when the performance was about
over, 'capturing third money.
In the final mile and a sixteenth run
Toribio, the favorite, made a runaway
race of It, heating Perseus two lengths
at the â¢Â¦wire -"in 1:46^. Tappan finished
third.
TRACK NOTES.
The attention of horsemen Is again
called to the fact that Harlem and Lake
side stake events close March 1. The
Saratoga Racing-Association's stakes also
close on the same date.
While being galloped on the track by a
forty-pound exercise boy yesterday morn
ing, Reolia became playful and ran away
ovt six miles with the little fellow. Â¦
McAuliffe, who had the mount on True
Blue, is an* Australian Jockey who was
seen in the saddle here when racing held
sway at the old Bay District track. It
was his first mount since returning >to
this country.

GENERAL BARNES HAS
JOINED THE BENEDICTS
Stole Away to New York With Mrs. Anna
Scott and Wedded Her.
His Bride Belongs to This City, but the Veteran
Clubman's Friends Will Hear the News
With Surprise.
POR days the genial face of General
Barnes has' been missing from his
accustomed haunts and among his
friends, and now comes the news
from the East that not only had he
pone to New York, but he traveled all
that way to be married. His bride is Mrs.
Anna M. Scott of this city.
Just why the veteran man about town
stole away to a distance of three thou
sand miles to be married and cheated a
thousand or two friends of the chance
to throw tons of rice and arrange in
numerable farewell stag dinners, is a
mystery. It may be that the sin of dif
fidence suddenly fell upon him and it may
be that he is getting unsociable as the
years accumulate, but whatever caused
It, all the reason he would give was that
he wished to be married in the city
Â¦where he was born and In the presence of
the brother he had not seen for twenty
five years..
The news cane over the wire last even
ing and it spread through the haunts of
Bohemia like a prairie fire. First there
was incredulity, then conviction and then
indignation at the first offense against
bociability the general has committed.
No one had the slightest idea what the
peneral was up to. He told* no one and
bore himself as a man proof against the
subtlest wiles of woman, but there cou!d
be no doubt of it: he had shaken his asso
t iates and had been married at a dis
tance, cheating his friends of the fairest
excuse for a Bohemian revel that has
happened In local clubdom.
Brought the Bride Along.
The general's wedding was character
istic. He went to New York and last
Monday dropped in on his brother, John
S. Barnes, at 22 East Forty-eighth
street. He told his brother he had come
all the way to be married, and to make
pure there would be no hitch in the cere
mony, he had brought the bride-to-be
along with him and he thereupon intro
duced Mrs. Scott to his astonished rela-
tive. The general explained why he
wished to be married in New York and
without more ado Rev. David H. Greer
of 342 Madison avenue was summoned
and in the presence of John S. Barnes
and his wife the general and Mrs. Scott
were married. The general said he did
not care for an elaborate ceremony nor
did he desire that the wedding be given
undue publicity, and he was very near
getting out of town and well on his way
home before the news got out at all, but
some one who knew what a rare item the
old general had cooked up got wind of the
affair and it was flashed across , to San
Francisco without delay.
After the ceremony General Barnes and
wife went to the Fifth Avenue Hotel and
yesterday morning they started for San
Francisco. . They will arrive here Sun
day or Monday.
She Was an Old Friend.
The new Mrs. Barnes Is an old friend of
the general. He knew her for years, and
her quick Intelligence and congenial men
tality attracted him always, and when the
late Mrs. Barnes was dying, something over
two years ago, Mrs. Scott was constantly
at the house, assisting in caring for the
sick woman. How long the wily old club
man was a- wooing no one knows. He never
told the story on himself, fond as he was
of telling stories, and he never hinted
that celibacy was anything but heaven
for him. He kept his own counsel and
there are those even now asking where
Barnes is and what has happened to him.
The marriage of the general is a dis
tinct event in more ways than ' one
in all the various circles the genial club
man crossed in his professional and social
orbit He Is one of the best known men
in the West and is one of the few who
have run the political gamut and left only
friends in his track. He was too good
natured to make much of a personal suc
cess cf politics, but he was an untiring
worker and there is hardly a hall In the
State that has not echoed his words of
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, FEBRUARY 28, 1900. \
Rumors of a Compromise Between
Mrs. Craven-Fair and the Fair
Heirs Follow Close Upon
Supreme Court Decision.
THE Supreme Court decision sustain
ing the "trust clause" of the Fair
will had a marked effect yesterday
on those engaged in the Craven-Fair
litigation now pending before Judge
Troutt. Mrs. Craven-Fair and her
counsel resumed with renewed zeal the
legal battle they are waging for a share
of the big estate, while the opposition felt
its way very carefully, in evident dread
of stepping on dangerous ground. Just
when their cross-examination of Mrs.
Craven-Fair had reached the very brink
of an exciting climax Attorney Pierson,
who has been plying the questions,
stopped short and announced that he was
through with the witness.
This was after a searching inquiry into
the Sausalito ceremony and the circum
stances which had prompted Justice
Simpton to tell his story in writing to At
torney Foote and swear later that it was
the truth. Questions had been asked
showing plainly that tbe attorneys on the
side of the estate were pursuing the
theory that the whole Sausalito story was
a fabrication which was the creation of
the fertile brain of the woman who claims
to be the widow of the late Senator Fair.
There had been some questioning earlier
in the cross-examination with reference
to the pencil will, and yesterday when the
time arrived to continue that course of in
quiry the fire of questions suddenly
ceased.
Rumors of Compromise.
This unexpected action, coming as it did
on top of the Supreme Court decision on
the "trust clause," started any amount of
speculation as to the whys and where
fores. The essence of all the rumors that
floated about during the afternoon and
evening was that a compromise had been
reached, that the Fair children had ln-
Flsted that the pencil will subject be
handled with extreme care until it was
determined definitely whether it might
not be the "open sesame" to the vast
wealth left by their father.
Mrs. Craven-Fair's attorneys scoffed at
the idea of a compromise, yet they admit
ted that they considered the surprising
turn of affairs in the light of a partial
surrender of the opposition and a most
decided evidence of weakness.
Mr. Pierson and his associates assert
that the sudden halt in the cross-examlra
tlon of Mrs. Craven-Fair has no signifi
cance whatever. He had gone as far as
he desired and quit, and that Is all there
Is to it. according to his assertions.

down. deÂ«plt#> the emphatic statements
from both sides that they Intend to con
test every inch of ground and -fight out

BRUTALLY ASSAULTED
IN HER OWN ROOM
Etta Fitzgibbon Is Attacked by a
Strange Man and Frightfully
Beaten.
While seated In her room in a lodging:
house ' at 17 Grant avenue la3t evening,
Etta Fitzgibbon, a middle-aged woman,
was attacked by a strange man and bru
tally beaten about the head and face with
some blunt instrument. After rendering
the woman unconscious the stranger ran
from the house, followed by an eye-wit
ness to the assault. Meeting Detectives
Dlnan and Wren, he excitedly told them
that the fleeing man had murdered a
woman.
The detectives gave chase, and. brlni?
unable to overtake the man, Dlnan drew
his revolver, and pointing it at the mur
derous assailant of the woman, pulled the
trigger. The cartridge failed to explode,
and as the fellow was fast outdistancing
his pursuers, Dlnan again pulled the trig
ger, with the same result. Replacing the
revolver In his pocket. Dlnan, who at one
time was a professional sprinter, discard
ed his overcoat and started after the fleet
footed stranger, determined to overtake
him. After an exciting chase of several
blocks he finally succeeded in overhauling
his game.
The stranger, seeing he was cornered,
suddenly turned on the detective, but was
placed hors de combat by a well directed
blow on the Jaw. At the City Prison he
gave his name as Charles Brown. HÂ» ad
mitted that he had beaten the Fitzgibbon
woman, but refused to assign any reason
for the cowardly assault.
With blood streaming from half a dozen
cuts in the head, the victim of Brown's
flendishness was at once removed to the
Receiving Hospital, where she was at
tended by Dr. Morrison. After the woutids
were dressed the woman, when ques
tioned declared that she did not know
her assailant and was at a loss to explain
why he had attacked her. About a week
ago she said she had a woman lodger in
the house arrested and she is inclined to
believe that her action in the matter
miKht have actuated tt\e assault. To-day
Brown will be formally charged with an
assault with a deadly weapon.
the issues Involved in the case If it takes
all summer. Mrs. Craven-Fair heraeif
says she was never more determined than
now to prove her case In court, and that
she will never quit until victory perciios
on her banner or death puts her out of
the battle. While she says the Supreme
Court decision dcÂ»s not concern her, she
cannot conceal the fact that she fliids
much about It to crow over. She expresses
great sympathy for the predicament in
which it has placed her "step-children,"
but H is hard to understand whether she
is prompted in making the assertion liy
sincerity or sarcasm. She certainly does
give <">ut the impression that If her "step
children" would agree to be good due
would forgive them for the trouble they
have put her to, and might lend them a
helping hand !n straightening out the af
fairs of the big estate.
And all this leads back again to the
question of compromise and the pencil
will. That the latter document is destine-1
to play an important part in the Craven-
Fair game cannot be questioned. Some
attorneys have expressed the opinion that
It is barred out by the statutes of limita
tion. These legal lights may be right,
but there are lawyers Â¦who. like love, can
rind a way for anything, and if it Is con
sidered necessary to bring forth the old
document again some legal loophole will
be found through which to pull it.
Simpton in a Bad Boat.
The proceedings yesterday before Judge
Troutt were rather more interesting than
usual and the crowd of loungers that
filled the courtroom were amply repaid
for their attendance. The story of the
Sausalito ceremony and all the circum
stances surrounding it was the subject
upon which the counsel for the estate
appeared most deeply interested and to
which they directed their cross-examina
tion of Mrs. Craven-Fair. Like the pre
vious day, however, they learned a little
more than they wanted to and got their
foot in it by making an opening through
which the pair of Pences forced the
Slmpton affidavit and letter on the erc
ord. Mr. McEnerney made a strenuous
effort to prevent this, for it Is bound to
prove embarrassing to both the represen
tatives of the estate and Simpton. It
makes his sworn statement of the Sausa
lito affair a part of the court proceedings,
to controvert the effect of which he will
have to be put on the witness stand to
tell his later yarn, in which he poses as
a hero engaged in trapping conspirators.
The Pences say this would constitute per
jury, and if it takes place they are going
to tell the District Attorney about it.
Simpton, evidently would like to be lost,
but he is still on Charlie Fair's launch off
the Sausalito shore and the question is:
Can his guardian afford to lose him? He
seems to be In a bad boat, even if It is
the Lucero.

Plans of Fair Heirs.
The heirs of the Fair estate have de
cided to continue the legal warfare to
set aside the "trust clause" in. the dead
mlllllonaire's will. The first move will
be to apply to the Supreme Court for a
rehearing. If this is not granted, Charlie
Fair's contest of the will, now pending
before Judge Troutt, will be taken up and
forced to a hearing, with the intention of
setting aside the entire testament.

Cheated Bohemia of a Revel.
It was not like him, and it is strange if
his injured friends, his professional
brethren and .his clubmates ; do not de
mand reparation of some full and satis
factory sort. His â¢ wedding would have
taken rank with the celebration of Uncle
George ; Bromley's birthday. His wooing
would have been, the pattern for, future
ventures of the same sort by hundreds of
his associates and his example would
have driven the clerk of the marriage
license office to work overtime and nights.
He has stolen a march on Bohemia and
isno doubt indulging in a- long-distance
chuckle over the prospect of cominghome
and saying, "It's all over." â¢
loyalty to the Republican party: and it
was seldom he spoke, when there, was not
something worth echoing. He has a rare
gift of oratory and it has made him
known quicker and better and farther
than ever politics could have done. He
has turned audiences of thousands from
laughter to tears, and from tears to the
wildest enthusiasm. He has been before
the public in every capacity for years and
has been the moving spirit of geniality
and Jollity and chummlness in the clubs,
and he was never known to flee from his
fellow-man under any sociable circum
stance until just the other day, when, he
slipped off to New York and deprived
clubdom of its choicest morsel of gossip.'

PLAN OF MUSEUM
NOW PERFECTED
Asiatic Association's Aid to
Be Accepted to Get
Large Trade.
Congress "Will Be Asked to Appropri
ate $50,000 for the Promotion.
of the Pacific Coast
-' Enterprise.
The Â¦ Padflc Commercial Museum has
now a plan of organization, .which was
adopted at a meeting of the committee on
promotion yesterday. Practically this is
the i plan which was formulated by the
committee of ten. What- now remains to
be done is to appoint a tinance committee
of sixty prominent citizens and then to
collect the funds necessary to actually
bring the museum into existence. This
committee on finance will be selected at
the next meeting of the promoters, which
will 'be -on -Friday afternoon next.
âÂ¦ A suggestion was made to change thÂ©
name on the ground that the people gen
erally thought that the purpose in view
was to establish a show of some sort. The
committee took the view that the purpose
would be clear to all who could be count
ed upon tt> assist. In addition to the adop
tion of the plan the promoters adopted a
resolution submitted by Isaac Upham and
amended " by M. H. de Young, . that the
home >project might come in for $50,000 of
Government aid, in which all commercial
bodies on the coast were asked to request
their Congressmen and Senators to sup
port the pending bill to appropriate $200,
000 to the Philadelphia Commercial Mu
seum. ,
Several proposed amendments to the
plan were rejected.
â¢An amendment was adopted, which was
Introduced by R. P. Schwerin, to co-op
erate with the American Asiatic Associa
tion. M. H." de Young opposed an amend
ment to take from the executive governor
the power to nominate all appointees and
to substitute an -executive committee of
nine with a manager to name the ap
pointees. He claimed that this would de
stroy the efficiency of the plan. The pro
moters took his view of the case and killed
the amendment. Every member of the
committee of promoters will submit names
for members of the finance committee at
the, next meeting.
Â¦ Isaac Upham's motion that President
Wheeler should be requested to call a
general meeting upon his return from the
East for the purpose of reporting his in
terview with President McKinley was
adopted. â¢
The features of the plan of organization
have -been published in The Call as they
were adopted by the promotion commit
tee.
